class="stElementContainer element-container st-emotion-cache-kj6hex eu6p4el1" data-testid="stElementContainer" data-stale="false" width="auto"><div class="stMarkdown" data-testid="stMarkdown"><div data-testid="stMarkdownContainer" class="st-emotion-cache-1104ytp e1icttdg0"><p>UNIT THREE PART ONE: LABOUR AND PUERPERIUM</p>
<p>You have successfully completed Units One and Two of this module on Reproductive Health. In the previous units, you learnt about obstetric anatomy, normal pregnancy, antenatal care, obstetric pharmacology and breastfeeding. As you learned, a well-managed pregnancy is likely to have a very happy ending. This unit will take you through labour, puerperium and the neonate.</p>
<p>The unit is divided into two parts. Part One deals with normal labour, the neonate and puerperium. In Part Two, we will discuss abnormal pregnancy and abnormal labour.</p>
<p>This unit is composed of two sections:</p>
<p>Section One: Normal Labour
Section Two: The Neonate</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="unit-objectives">Unit Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this unit you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe the process of normal labour</li>
<li>Describe the management of mother during puerperium</li>
<li>Describe the management of the newborn during neonatal period</li>
<li>Identify neonatal complications, their management and prevention</li>
<li>Apply the knowledge acquired in the management of a woman during labour and puerperium</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="section-1-normal-labour">SECTION 1: NORMAL LABOUR<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-1-normal-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Your role as a midwife demands clinical expertise in supervising, caring for and supporting the pregnant mother during pre-pregnancy, pregnancy, labour and puerperium. You should be able to conduct deliveries on your own, and be ready to intervene when complications arise. It is also your responsibility to recognise and act promptly should you be presented with an abnormal condition, for example: mal-presentation, obstructed labour, or obstetric and neonatal emergencies.</p>
<p>In order to recognise and deal with complications of pregnancy, delivery, and the puerperium, you as a health worker must first be thoroughly familiar with the characteristics of normal pregnancy, delivery and puerperium. As you may remember, you have already discussed normal pregnancy in the previous Unit. In this Unit, you will review the features of normal and abnormal labour and consider how each stage of labour is managed. You will also examine the normal puerperium and review methods of monitoring a mother for post-delivery complications.</p>
<p>Do you know that a mother, who is psychologically well prepared during pre-natal care, will go through labour and delivery with ease? Research has shown that if a woman in labour has confidence in her caregiver she will experience a considerably lower level of labour pains. Professionalism, calmness and a 'matter-of-fact' manner on the part of the caregiver is a greater tranquiliser to the mother who is in pain than medicine (Leeder: 1994).</p>
<p>It goes without saying, therefore, that a midwife's behaviour and attitude towards a mother in labour makes the difference between painful and less-painful labour. A relaxed mother will usually have a shorter period of labour than a tense mother.</p>
<p>Before we proceed, let us look at the objectives of this section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this unit you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Define labour</li>
<li>Explain the onset of labour</li>
<li>Identify the three stages of labour</li>
<li>Describe the physiological changes that occur during labour</li>
<li>Describe the management of normal labour</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="labour">Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The word labour is commonly used by both medical and non-medical people, but what does this term mean to you as a midwife?</p>
<p>Labour is described as the process whereby the foetus, placenta and membranes are expelled through the birth canal after 28 weeks of gestation. Labour, can be either normal or abnormal.</p>
<p>Normal labour has several important characteristics, which you should always remember.</p>
<p>These are:</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 94 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Duration - completed within 18 hours</li>
<li>Occurs at term between 38 and 40 weeks</li>
<li>Is spontaneous</li>
<li>The foetus presents by the vertex</li>
<li>Has no complications to either mother or baby</li>
<li>The newborn child requires minimal or no resuscitation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-onset-of-labour">The Onset of Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-onset-of-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A midwife should ensure women have sufficient information to assist them to recognise the onset of true labour. A pregnant woman would be best placed to diagnose the onset of labour herself.</p>
<p>Some young women, especially the primigravidae (those pregnant for the first time), may fail to recognise true labour. It is important that you help them differentiate between false and true labour signs. The contractions of true labour are regular and intense. In false labour, the contractions are sporadic (Braxton-Hicks contractions). False contractions occur during the last weeks of pregnancy. The following table outlines some factors that can help to differentiate true and false labour.</p>
<p>The presence of the following signs and symptoms will give evidence that the mother is in labour:</p>
<ul>
<li>Contractions of the uterus, which are increasingly strong, painful and regular</li>
<li>The cervix is taken up into the lower uterine segment causing dilatation of the cervix</li>
<li>There is a mucoid blood stained discharge, which is called show</li>
<li>Sometimes there is rupture of membranes with drainage of liquor amnii (amniotic fluid)</li>
</ul>
<p>The causes that trigger the onset of labour are not known. However, many theories have been offered which indicate that both hormonal and mechanical factors play a big part. You will now study each of these factors in turn.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-contrast-between-true-labour-and-false-labour">The Contrast Between True Labour and False Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-contrast-between-true-labour-and-false-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Factors</th><th>True Labour</th><th>False Labour</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Contractions</td><td>Regularly spaced</td><td>Irregularly spaced</td></tr><tr><td>Interval between contractions</td><td>Gradually shortens</td><td>Remains long</td></tr><tr><td>---</td><td>---</td><td>---</td></tr><tr><td>Intensity of contractions</td><td>Gradually increases</td><td>Stays the same</td></tr><tr><td>Location of pain</td><td>Back and abdomen</td><td>Mostly lower abdomen</td></tr><tr><td>Effect of analgesics</td><td>Do not abolish the pain</td><td>Often abolish the pain</td></tr><tr><td>Cervical changes</td><td>Progressive effacement and dilatation</td><td>No changes</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hormonal-factors">Hormonal Factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hormonal-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is believed that close to term progesterone levels in the body fall, while at the same time levels of oestrogen (which is responsible for sensitising the uterine muscles) rise. The fall in progesterone levels is important because it has effect on muscle contractions. The rise in oestrogen levels meanwhile triggers the release of oxytocin, which causes uterine contractions. The foetal hypothalamus is believed to produce releasing factors, which stimulate the anterior pituitary gland to produce adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH). ACTH stimulates the foetal adrenal glands to secrete cortisol, which causes relative levels of placental hormones to rise. These cause further uterine contractions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanical-factors">Mechanical Factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanical-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Uterine activity can also result from the mechanical stimulation of the uterus and cervix. This may be due to over stretching, as in the case of multiple pregnancy and polyhydromnios, or pressure from the presenting part, when it is well applied to the cervix.</p>
<p>It appears that there is a combination of hormonal (from both mother and foetus) and mechanical factors that set labour in motion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pre-labour-or-premonitory-signs-of-labour">Pre-Labour or Premonitory Signs of Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pre-labour-or-premonitory-signs-of-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the period two to three weeks prior to the onset of labour when a number of changes take place. You will now look at these changes in greater detail.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 95 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 96 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lightening">Lightening<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lightening" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Two to three weeks before labour, the lower uterine segment expands allowing the foetal head to sink deep. The descent of the head and the body of the baby gives space to the lungs, heart and stomach, which enables these organs to function easily.</p>
<p>The symphysis pubis widens and the pelvic floor softens and becomes more relaxed, allowing further descent of the uterus into the pelvis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="frequency-of-micturition">Frequency of Micturition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frequency-of-micturition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The descent of the foetal head increases pressure within the pelvis. This limits the capacity of the bladder, which can cause irritation. The laxity of the pelvic floor muscles gives rise to poor sphincter control causing a degree of stress incontinence. This pressure results in the congestion of circulation to the lower limbs. Additionally the relaxation of the pelvic joint may give rise to backache.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="taking-up-of-cervix">Taking up of Cervix<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#taking-up-of-cervix" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The cervix is taken up gradually and merges into the lower uterine segment. Shortening of cervix is looked for when labour needs to be induced.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="contractions">Contractions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contractions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The contractions of the uterus are coordinated by two pacemakers in the region of the cornua. These are located where the fallopian tubes join the uterine body. The muscle contractions start at the top corner of the uterus, spread to the fundus, and then downward. During normal pregnancy, the uterus contracts intermittently but the contractions are not strong enough to overcome the resistance of a normal cervix and do not lead to its dilation. The contractions of pregnancy become more frequent towards full term and get more painful and noticeable. A multipara may have such 'false pains' for some days before the onset of true labour. They may come to hospital too early thinking they are in established labour. This is what is referred to as 'false labour'.</p>
<p>You will now look at uterine action and how contractions interact with the cervix.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="uterine-action">Uterine Action<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#uterine-action" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>By the end of pregnancy, the uterus is divided into two anatomically distinct segments, known as the upper and the lower uterine segments.</p>
<p>The upper uterine segment is a thick muscular, contractile area from where the contractions begin. The longitudinal fibres retract, pulling on the lower segment and causing it to stretch, pushing the head down.</p>
<p>The lower uterine segment is thinner and develops from the isthmus of the uterus about eight to ten centimetres in length and is prepared for distension and/or dilatation. The lower segment stretches when being pulled by the longitudinal fibres. The force applied by the descending head or breech also aids the stretching.</p>
<p>The retraction ring which is an imaginary ridge, forms between the upper and the lower uterine segment. It is present in every labour and is perfectly normal as long as it is not marked enough to be visible above the symphysis pubis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fundal-dominance">Fundal Dominance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fundal-dominance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>During a contraction the uterus feels hard to touch. At the beginning of the process, contractions are painless and involuntary, and are controlled by the nervous system under the influence of endocrine hormones.</p>
<p>The contraction starts at the upper part of fundus, spreading across, and by the time they reach the lower fundus, they last longer and are very intense. The peak of the contraction is reached simultaneously over the whole uterus and fades from all parts together. This pattern allows the cervix to dilate and the contracting fundus to expel the foetus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="polarity">Polarity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#polarity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Polarity describes the neuromuscular harmony between the two poles or segments of the uterus throughout labour. The upper pole contracts strongly and retracts to expel the foetus. The lower pole contracts slightly and dilates to allow expulsion of the foetus to take place.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="contraction-and-retraction">Contraction and Retraction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraction-and-retraction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When labour starts, approximately 280 days from the first day of the last menstrual period, the contractions change in character. They become regular and more painful. Labour contractions differ from those of pregnancy in that they are followed by retraction. This is characteristic of uterine muscle in labour. The contracted muscle does not return to its original length when the contraction passes off.</p>
<p>Each succeeding contraction leads to further shortening of the muscle fibres so that the uterine cavity becomes smaller and smaller.</p>
<p>This is what makes the cervix dilate. This process is illustrated in the figure below.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-a351d027.jpeg" alt="img-0.jpeg"></p>
<p>When talking about contractions, you as a midwife are concerned with three factors, namely the strength, the duration and the frequency of the contraction.</p>
<p>When you talk of the strength of a contraction, you identify it as one of three categories: weak, fair or fairly strong, and strong. The strength of a contraction is measured according to the time it has taken. Thus, a contraction which takes 10 to 30 seconds is said to be weak, one that takes 30 to 40 is said to be fair or fairly strong and one that lasts for 40 to 60 seconds is said to be strong.</p>
<p>The duration refers to the time taken by a contraction, for example a weak contraction lasts for 10 to 30 seconds.</p>
<p>Frequency, on the other hand refers to the number of intervals between one contraction and the next. If a mother has one contraction after every 45 minutes, the frequency is written as 1:45.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-shortening-and-dilation-of-the-cervix">The Shortening and Dilation of The Cervix<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-shortening-and-dilation-of-the-cervix" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Before labour begins, the cervix of a primagravida is a thick hard cone which protrudes into the vagina. The canal is at least one inch long. When labour begins the strongly contracting upper segment of the uterus starts retracting and getting shorter, while the thinner lower segment of the uterus gets pulled away from the presenting part. This stretches the lower segment. The latter, in turn pulls the internal Os. This dragging away of the internal Os from the presenting part starts dilating the upper part of the cervical canal. This goes on until the canal is shorter and shorter and finally there is no canal at all. The canal becomes part of the uterine cavity with only the undilated</p>
<p>external Os and the thinly stretched cervix separating this cavity from the vagina. When this happens, we say the cervix has been 'effaced' or 'taken up'.</p>
<p>Click the links to reveal the stages of the effacement of the cervix:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-cd9eba8f.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg">
Cervical canal before effacement</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-513d540d.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg">
Partial effacement
Effacement almost complete</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 97 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-ce440753.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
Effacement fully complete</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-c83419e6.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg"></p>
<p>In a primigravida the cervix usually becomes almost fully effaced before any dilation takes place, while in multiparous women the two processes take place together. The cervix of a multipara might be already effaced and be dilated enough to admit a finger up to the internal Os even before labour starts. These signs of labour are assessed by doing a vaginal examination, though with experience, you can also get a good idea by doing a rectal examination.</p>
<p>The way you guide a mother has a great influence on the progress of her labour.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-show">The Show<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-show" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Throughout pregnancy the cervical canal is sealed by a plug of mucus known as an operculum. Together with the intact membranes this prevents organisms ascending into the uterine cavity.</p>
<p>When labour starts, the internal Os is pulled away from the foetal membranes and the canal is opened up. This releases the mucous plug which oozes out of the vagina mixed with a little blood. This is called the 'show'.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-stages-of-labour">The Stages of Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-stages-of-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Labour is divided into four stages, although in real practice, the process is a continuous one and change from one stage to the other may not be clearly obvious. The four stages of labour are known as First stage, Second stage, Third stage and Fourth stage.</p>
<p>Each stage will now be covered in detail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-first-stage">The First Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-first-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is known as the stage of cervical dilatation. This stage begins when regular, painful uterine contractions start and is detected clinically by the thinning and effacement of the cervix, followed by its dilatation. The normally thick cervix becomes thinned out and stretched over the presenting part. The first stage is completed when the cervix is fully dilated and the presenting part starts being expelled. This stage has two phases, known as the latent and the active phase.</p>
<p>The contractions of the uterus dilate the cervix. The dilatation of the internal Os causes the separation of the chorion from the decidua closest to it. A small bag of membranes is formed and is forced into the internal Os by the intrauterine pressure. At the beginning of each contraction, a little more amniotic fluid is forced into the bag of membranes. The head of the foetus then comes down like a ball valve and separates the amniotic fluid above it from that in the bag. The bag of membranes may remain intact until nearly the end of the first stage. However, even if the membranes rupture early the cervix will still become dilated as it is drawn up over the presenting part by the retraction of the upper segment.</p>
<p>During the first stage, the foetus does not move downwards to any great degree. When a certain amount of fluid has left the uterus after the membranes have ruptured, a new form of pressure comes into play, namely foetal axis pressure. The upper pole of the foetus, normally the breech, is pressed on by the fundus of the uterus, while the lower pole is pressed down onto the lower segment and cervix. Should the membranes rupture early, foetal axis pressure will operate at an early stage. In modern practice, the membranes are often deliberately ruptured during labour because this is believed to encourage more efficient uterine action and shorten labour. You should be cautious carrying out this procedure in this era of HIV.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 98 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>The duration of the latent phase of labour need not be defined too accurately. Dilation of the cervix from 0 to 3cm can take six hours, but slower progress may be normal and is perfectly acceptable provided that the woman is comfortable and in no way distressed. Between 3 and 10cm dilatation (that is in the active phase of labour) the cervix should dilate at a rate of about 1 cm per hour, giving a theoretical duration of seven hours for this phase of labour in both primiparous and multiparous women. During the early part of the latent phase the pains may not be very severe but towards the end of the active phase they are often very distressing, constituting the most painful part of labour. Vomiting and reflex shivering are common at the end of the active phase of the first stage of labour.</p>
<p>By now the membranes have ruptured. If the membranes remain intact when the cervix is fully dilated, the onset of the expulsive stage may be delayed. This is because the cervix does not receive the pressure of the head, which helps to stimulate the uterus to increase its activity. If the membranes remain intact after full dilatation, they should be ruptured with toothed forceps or a sterile plastic amnihook during a contraction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="second-stage">Second Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#second-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This starts with the full dilatation of the cervix and</p>
<p>the expulsion of the presenting part and finishes with the complete delivery of the baby. As you know,</p>
<p>there may be very little descent of the foetus during</p>
<p>the first stage. However, in the second stage, the</p>
<p>resistance offered by the lower uterine segment and</p>
<p>the cervix has been overcome and the presenting</p>
<p>part can be pushed down onto the pelvic floor. The resistance of the pelvic floor then has to be overcome by uterine contractions, aided by the action of the voluntary muscles of the abdominal wall and the diaphragm.</p>
<p>In the absence of an effective epidural block, full dilatation of the cervix is accompanied by a bearing down sensation during contractions and women are usually encouraged to push. As the contraction comes on, the woman takes a deep breath, then holds it and subsequently bears down with all the force of her abdominal muscles. These partly voluntary, partly reflex</p>
<p>expulsive efforts place the foetus under additional stress and pushing should, therefore, not be allowed to continue for more than one hour. If delivery is not imminent, assistance in the form of Ventouse extraction, forceps delivery or even a Caesarean section may be necessary.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="third-stage">Third Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#third-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This stage commences immediately after the birth of the baby. It includes the delivery of the placenta and membranes as well as the control of bleeding. At this stage the uterus contracts down to follow the body of the foetus as it is being born. As the cavity of the uterus becomes smaller, the area of the placental site is diminished. The placenta is then cut off from the spongy layer of the decidua basalis. Further uterine contractions expel the placenta from the upper segment into the lower segment and through the vaginal vault. This process, whereby the placenta leaves the upper segment to the lower segment and through the vagina, is referred to as separation and descent.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fourth-stage">Fourth Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fourth-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the period from the delivery of placenta and membranes to the end of the first hour postpartum. The uterus is firm at level of two fingers breadth above the umbilicus. Restoration of physiological stability is established. During this period myometrial contractions and retraction, accompanied by vessel thrombosis, operate effectively to control bleeding from the placenta site. Failure of this mechanism could result in excessive blood loss (postpartum haemorrhage (PPH)) that could be life threatening. The mother should be closely observed for haemorrhage, urine retention or hypotension. The mother and child relationship should be initiated and encouraged, as it has an effect to the subsequent quality of their relationship and bonding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physiological-changes-in-labour">Physiological Changes in Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physiological-changes-in-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You will continue with the study of the four stages of labour, paying particular attention to the physiological and mechanical changes that are involved.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 99 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="physiological-changes-in-the-first-stage-of-labour">Physiological Changes in the First Stage of Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physiological-changes-in-the-first-stage-of-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is the stage of dilatation of the cervix. On average, it lasts eight to twelve hours in a primigravida and six to eight hours in a multipara. It should not go beyond 14 hours in either. This stage is characterised by the uterus doing an immense amount of muscular work in the form of contracting and relaxing. Contractions are involuntary in that they do not come on through voluntary effort of the woman nor can they be voluntarily stopped. They are peristaltic and regular.</p>
<p>Contractions at the start of labour come every 10 to 15 minutes and last between 30 to 45 seconds. They increase in frequency and strength as labour progresses until they are separated by a minute or two. Towards the end of the first stage they last for one minute. The relaxation phase during which the muscles remain retracted also shortens.</p>
<p>Each contraction begins with a gradual build-up towards a peak of intensity. This is followed by a relaxation phase. During the relaxation phase, the muscle recovers and gets ready for the next contraction. This relaxation phase is important to both the foetus and the mother during the first and second stages of labour.</p>
<p>During a contraction the circulation through the uterine wall is reduced and the foetal heart rate is slowed but regains its normal rate as soon as the contraction has passed. If there is increasing foetal tachycardia between contractions or if the bradycardia is prolonged after each contraction, then foetal distress sets in. If the uterus contracts continuously the foetus dies from lack of oxygen (anoxia).</p>
<p>The mother is also able to relax during the relaxation phase. However, if the uterus contracts continuously, the mother also gets very exhausted because the uterus uses up a lot of energy during contractions. If this goes on for too long, as is the case during prolonged labour, her energy stores become depleted and maternal distress sets in.</p>
<p>As the mother will not be able to eat or absorb much by mouth, she should be given supplementary carbohydrates intravenously. In a normal first stage, oral fluids to which additional sugar has been added are sufficient.</p>
<p>As the uterus contracts and retracts more and more, the upper muscular part becomes progressively thicker. The less muscular lower segment is pulled upwards over the presenting part and becomes thinner. The cervix becomes effaced. The effacement is followed or accompanied by progressive dilation of the cervix until full dilatation when the uterus becomes a continuous cavity with the vagina.</p>
<p>A fully dilated cervix is 10 cm dilated. During the first stage the uterine cavity gets progressively smaller but the foetus moves down very little. The presenting part helps in dilating the cervix. During this stage the woman should not use her voluntary efforts to bear down as this will exhaust her unnecessarily and may cause oedema of the cervix and/or foetal distress.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-normal-labour-in-the-admission-room">Management of Normal Labour in the Admission Room<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-normal-labour-in-the-admission-room" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The proper management of labour is essential, if you are to avoid problems or to detect them early when they occur. The patient will come to you believing she is in labour. You should be able to assess and decide whether she is in labour or not. The patient may be in early labour, but often she might arrive in the late second or even third stage.</p>
<p>If you are sure she is not in labour send her home to wait. If she is in labour, keep her in the ward and continue monitoring her progress. Danger, especially to the foetus, can arise suddenly and unexpectedly</p>
<p><strong>No labour should be assumed normal until the four stage has successfully concluded.</strong></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="admission">Admission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#admission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When you admit a mother in the first stage of labour, what activities should you carry out?</p>
<p>You would take the patient's personal history, conduct a physical examination and carry out tests/investigations.</p>
<p>On admission, you should check the woman's antenatal card for any identified risk factors. It will also help you to see if there were any abnormalities during her pregnancy. The records will also have information on her medical and obstetric history. If she has not been attending an antenatal clinic, this is the time to take detailed history as covered in unit two of this module.</p>
<p>Once this information has been established, find out more about the present labour. Take her history, do an examination and carry out the necessary investigations to establish the stage of labour and the state of the mother and the</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 100 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>foetus. The steps you should take will be covered on the following pages.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="history-taking">History Taking<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#history-taking" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A detailed personal history should have been taken during pre-natal care. However, if this has not been done, this is a good time to get it recorded. Make sure the names are correctly spelled because this can eventually result in problems when registering the baby. Review the last day of menstruation to calculate the expected date of delivery. Check her age, parity and contraceptive history. Assuming that a detailed personal history had been taken during pre-natal care, you should now take information about the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Any presence of show</li>
<li>Presence or absence of contractions</li>
<li>Onset of contractions and their characteristics</li>
<li>Activity of the foetus</li>
<li>Rupture of the membranes</li>
<li>Any treatment given</li>
<li>Food taken in the last four hours</li>
</ul>
<p>After taking the personal history, there are some observations that you should make.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="can-you-think-what-these-might-be">Can you think what these might be?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#can-you-think-what-these-might-be" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Assess the general condition and progress of labour in the mother, as well as vital signs and blood pressure. Test urine for protein, glucose and ketones and report any abnormalities.</p>
<p>After taking the history and observing the mother you now need to take carry out a thorough physical and vaginal examination.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="head-to-toe-physical-examination">Head to Toe Physical Examination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#head-to-toe-physical-examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should start by explaining to the mother that you want to examine her. The health care provider should appreciate the psychological aspect of a woman in labour, respect her feelings and the need for company or privacy. They should support the woman and her partner or family during labour, birth and the immediate postpartum period. Failure to do this has contributed to many women delivering at home, a place where they are appreciated but without an assured clean and safe delivery.</p>
<p>Can you remember how to examine the mother systematically?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-to-examine-the-mother-systematically">How to Examine the Mother Systematically<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-to-examine-the-mother-systematically" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When examining her, check on her general condition. Check if she is exhausted, anaemic, in great pain, dehydrated, or with generalised oedema. You should also check her height. This will enable you to exclude any risk factors.</p>
<p>You should also take her vital measurements including her blood pressure, pulse, temperature, and respiratory rate. Conduct an abdominal examination checking for:</p>
<ul>
<li>Height of fundus</li>
<li>Over-distension of the abdomen, scars or other abnormality</li>
<li>Over-distension of bladder</li>
<li>Possible presence of twins or multiple pregnancy</li>
<li>Contractions - frequency, length, type and strength</li>
<li>Lie of foetus - this is the relation of the long axis of the foetus to the long axis of the uterus (it can be longitudinal, oblique or transverse)</li>
<li>Rate and rhythm of the foetal heart</li>
</ul>
<p>You should also check on the presentation. Which part of the foetus is at the pelvic brim? Is it a head (cephalic) or the buttocks (breech)? Check the attitude; whether the head is well flexed or extended. A well-flexed head presents the smallest diameter and delivers easily. A deflexed head presents a larger diameter and causes delayed or obstructed labour.</p>
<p>Finally check the position of the relation of the foetal parts to the mother. This is confirmed through a vaginal examination by checking the position of the foetal occiput relative to the mother. The position of the foetal spine is the same as that of the occiput.</p>
<p>Refer to unit two for a more detailed description of this systematic examination. After a thorough head-to-toe examination you should perform a vaginal examination.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vaginal-examination-in-labour">Vaginal Examination in Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vaginal-examination-in-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is an important examination as it can give you a lot of information, which you might not get from an abdominal examination. On the other hand, if you do it often it is uncomfortable for the woman and you might introduce an infection into the uterine cavity, especially if the membranes have ruptured. To avoid infections, you should scrub and put on gloves as you would for any</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers
Page 101 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>other sterile procedure. Then thoroughly swab the perineum of the woman with an antiseptic solution such as Savlon or Hibitane, or boiled water if these are not available.</p>
<p>A vaginal examination is necessary to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Check if the patient is in labour and what stage of labour (this is done on admission of the patient)</li>
<li>Assess the progress of labour (this is done every four hours during the first stage of labour)</li>
<li>The degree of effacement and dilatation of the cervix and the station of the head relative to the ischial spines will give you the necessary information</li>
<li>Check that there is no prolapse of the cord when the membranes rupture</li>
</ul>
<p>Remember: Do not do a vaginal examination if the mother has an ante-partuhaemorrhage, because if there is placenta praevia, severe haemorrhage will occur.</p>
<p>A vaginal examination is contraindicated if the mother has ante-partum haemorrhage unless it is performed in an operating theatre. In case of pre-eclampsia, the procedure should be performed only after giving a sedative.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vaginal-examination">Vaginal Examination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vaginal-examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Arrange your vaginal examination pack with cheatle forceps and pour solution.</li>
<li>Scrub your hands for at least five minutes.</li>
<li>Glove yourself methodically to prevent contamination.</li>
<li>Explain the semi-lithotomy position that should be maintained during the examination to the mother.</li>
<li>Swab the vulva and drop the swab methodically (used swabs should be decontaminated in jik before disposal).</li>
<li>Ask the mother to breathe in and out while you perform digital examination.</li>
<li>With the right hand, gently insert the fingers obliquely inside the vagina with the thumb, facing the symphysis pubis. Your left hand should be on the mother's abdomen.</li>
<li>The fingers to be introduced are held on a higher level than the vaginal orifice during insertion to avoid contact with the anus. Fingers should not be withdrawn</li>
</ul>
<p>until the required information has been obtained.</p>
<ul>
<li>The fingers are directed along the anterior wall of the vagina. The wall should feel soft and dilatable while the vagina should be warm and moist.</li>
<li>The fingers are then directed upwards to the position of the cervical Os.</li>
<li>At times the Os is not felt readily, the fingers should then be directed backwards and upwards.</li>
</ul>
<p>While performing a vaginal examination, you should observe the mother's non-verbal communication.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-vagina">The Vagina<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-vagina" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="the-cervix">The Cervix<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-cervix" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Is it bruised or oedematous?</li>
<li>Is it firm or soft?</li>
<li>Is it taking up, that is effaced?</li>
<li>How much is the Os dilated?</li>
</ul>
<p>Do not insert more than two fingers in the cervical Os.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-membranes">The Membranes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-membranes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>After deciding the state of the cervical Os, check for presence of membranes. Note the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Are they ruptured or intact?</li>
<li>If intact are they bulging?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="the-cord">The Cord<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-cord" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Is it presenting or prolapsed?</li>
<li>If prolapsed is it pulsating?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-presenting-part">The Presenting Part<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-presenting-part" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Next, determine the level of the presenting part. You should ask the following questions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Is it fitting the pelvis and cervix well?</li>
<li>If it is the head, can you feel a suture or fontanel? Which one?</li>
<li>Is it well flexed?</li>
<li>If the head is at the brim it, will not be felt vaginally unless you push it down with your left hand which is on the mother's abdomen</li>
<li>If the head has just engaged, it can be touched or just be tipped</li>
<li>If the head is deeply engaged, the head is felt at the level of the ischial spines</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>While performing a vaginal examination, you should observe the mother's non-verbal communication.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-presenting-part">The Presenting Part<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-presenting-part" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The station or level of the presenting part is the level to which the presenting part has descended in the pelvis. The level of the presenting part is expressed in relation to the easily palpable ischial spines. You can also state if it is above the brim, at the brim, in the cavity or at the outlet.</p>
<p>Remember that if the presenting part is at the brim then it is at station -5. As the head descends down in to the cavity of the pelvis it decreases from -5, -4, -3, -2, -1. By the time it is at the ischial spines, it is said to be at 'zero station'. It reappears from the pelvic outlet into the perineum, which is classified as +1, +2, +3, and +4. By the time it is seen at the perineum it is at station +5.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-df7635b0.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="observe-the-position-of-the-presenting-part-this-is-the-position-of-the-foetal-parts-in-relation-to-the-parts-of-the-pelvis-a-point-on-the-foetus-such-as-the-occiput-in-a-vertex-presentation-is-usually-used-as-a-reference-point">Observe the position of the presenting part. This is the position of the foetal parts in relation to the parts of the pelvis. A point on the foetus, such as the occiput in a vertex presentation, is usually used as a reference point.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#observe-the-position-of-the-presenting-part-this-is-the-position-of-the-foetal-parts-in-relation-to-the-parts-of-the-pelvis-a-point-on-the-foetus-such-as-the-occiput-in-a-vertex-presentation-is-usually-used-as-a-reference-point" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To get the position right, you have to palpate the sutures and fontanel to determine their position relative to the pelvis. This can be confirmed by abdominal examination.</p>
<p>In a breech or a face presentation, the reference points on the foetus are the sacrum and the chin or mentum respectively. A soft mass is felt. Foetal genital parts or the anus may be felt with the examining finger. The position is determined by the position of the sacrum in relation to the side of the mother's pelvis. The left side is expressed as left sacro-anterior (LSA) or the right side, which is known as right sacro-anterior (RSA).</p>
<p>In a vertex presentation, when the occiput is persistently posterior, this causes prolonged labour. In a cephalic presentation, you will feel the hard head sutures and fontanel. Determine whether it is the anterior or posterior fontanel by its shape. If it is the posterior fontanel, then the position is occipito-anterior. If it is the anterior fontanel then the position is occipital-posterior.</p>
<p>Face presentation is important as the chin is the facial landmark. Note how the position of the chin is related to the mother's right thigh (RT) or left thigh (LT), side of the pelvis and the anterior and posterior position expressed as RMP (right mento posterior). You should also note the brow or sincipit presentation on vaginal examination (VE). You should be able to feel the brow, frontal sutures and possibly the posterior fontanel. The extension is usually a temporary presentation, which converts to face or occiput presentation during labour. However, at times it persists and in such instances, a caesarean section is usually called for.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="illustration-of-baby-in-mothers-womb-showing-normal-presentation">Illustration of baby in mothers womb showing normal presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#illustration-of-baby-in-mothers-womb-showing-normal-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-6bba04ff.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg"></p>
<p>Note whether the long axis of the body of the foetus is perpendicular to that of the body of the mother. A left acromiodorso posterior (LADP) indicates that the baby's lower shoulder is to the</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 103 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>mother's left and its back is towards her back. A compound presentation is when the hand or occasionally a foot lies alongside the head.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-pelvis">The Pelvis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-pelvis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Next check for moulding or caput succedaneum.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-moulding">What is moulding?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-moulding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Moulding is when the diameters of the foetal skull are reduced in size. During labour the bones of the foetal skull tend to overlap at the sutures so that the head can easily pass through the birth canal. During a vaginal examination this is how you should check for moulding:</p>
<ul>
<li>In cephalic presentation, run the finger on the head feeling for the sutures</li>
<li>Judge the degree of moulding by feeling the amount of overlapping of skull bones</li>
<li>Check for caput</li>
</ul>
<p>The pelvis is assessed to check if it is adequate (see unit two).</p>
<p>The following factors should be checked:</p>
<ul>
<li>Is it roomy?</li>
<li>Are the sidewalls well spaced?</li>
<li>Can you touch the promontory of the sacrum easily?</li>
<li>Is the pubic arch wide enough?</li>
<li>After checking for moulding, direct the fingers behind the head and make an effort to reach the sacro-promontory. The palm of the hand should be facing upwards. Promontory of the sacrum should not be reached.</li>
<li>With the palm facing downwards run the two fingers down along the hollow of the sacrum and determine the shape. The hollow of the sacrum should be curved.</li>
<li>The two fingers are then moved to where the ischial spines are located on either side of the pelvis. Run the finger along this area to determine whether or not the ischial spines are unduly prominent. Or else you can stretch your fingers to see if the spines are prominent. The ischial spines should not be prominent.</li>
<li>As you move the fingers with the palm facing upward, on reaching the pubic arch check if it can accommodate two fingers. The apex usually should accommodate two fingers.</li>
<li>Make a fist facing downwards then place the fist between the ischial tuberosities. The Intertuberosity should accommodate four knuckles. Check if it does.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-discharge">The Discharge<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-discharge" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Withdraw the fingers and check if there is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Any vaginal discharge</li>
<li>Any smell</li>
<li>Any liquor or meconium staining</li>
<li>Any bleeding</li>
</ul>
<p>The following steps should be taken as part of your investigation:</p>
<ul>
<li>Take a urine sample for albumin and sugar</li>
<li>Check for acetone, especially if the patient is in prolonged labour</li>
<li>Take blood for haemoglobin and cross matching if the patient is anaemic or might need an operation</li>
</ul>
<p>By this time you will have gathered enough information as to the stage of labour and whether the patient belongs to the 'at risk' category and needs referral or not.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-first-stage-of-labour">Management of First Stage of Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-first-stage-of-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When managing the first stage of labour, you should keep to the following procedure, making sure you record your findings in a partogram:</p>
<ul>
<li>Admit the patient to the waiting room, reassure her and introduce her to other patients</li>
<li>Reassure her and explain what is being done at every stage</li>
<li>Give her an enema only if she is in early labour (this will reduce the risk of faecal soiling and infection at delivery)</li>
<li>The patient may have a warm bath and change into a hospital gown</li>
<li>Encourage her to walk about and empty her bladder frequently</li>
<li>Give her plenty of fluids with sugar or glucose as she has to work hard and needs the energy</li>
<li>Do not allow any solid foods as the stomach takes a long time to empty in labour</li>
</ul>
<p>Should she need an operation and anaesthesia, the emptying of the stomach will be difficult and she might vomit and inhale the vomit.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>If she is in much pain and the delivery is still far off, give her a sedative.</p>
<p>Check the following regularly:</p>
<ul>
<li>Check the foetal heart rate half hourly or more often if you suspect distress</li>
<li>Check uterine contractions (strength, type, frequency and duration) as well as maternal pulse, BP and temperature</li>
<li>Check the urine output and check for albumin and acetone if indicated every two hours</li>
<li>Every four hours check the level of the presenting part and the degree of dilatation of the cervix</li>
<li>Constantly check the woman's reaction to labour and be aware of her needs, especially for pain relief. You can repeat pethidine 50 mg IM if cervical dilatation is still 5 cm or less. Do not give more pethidine if delivery is imminent as it depresses the baby's respiration</li>
<li>Towards the end of the first stage, she can rest on her side, or in any position she finds comfortable, for example, squatting</li>
<li>Discourage pushing or bearing down before the cervix is fully dilated</li>
<li>Early pushing only exhausts the woman and will cause oedema of the cervix and interfere with normal dilatation</li>
<li>If the bladder is full and she cannot empty it on her own, catheterise her using aseptic technique</li>
<li>When the membranes rupture, usually at the end of the first stage, check the colour of the liquor for meconium staining, the foetal heart rate and do a vaginal examination to exclude prolapse of the cord</li>
</ul>
<p>Write down the observations you are supposed to record in the partogram?</p>
<ul>
<li>Vital signs</li>
<li>Blood pressure</li>
<li>Details of vaginal examination</li>
<li>Contraction strength and number of contractions in ten minutes</li>
<li>Fluid balance</li>
<li>Urinalysis</li>
<li>Drugs administered</li>
</ul>
<p>When observing the contractions, you should note the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Uterine contraction duration, strength and frequency</li>
<li>In early labour the contractions are mild, lasting 20 to 30 seconds and are infrequent</li>
<li>As labour progresses, the contractions become stronger, lasting 40 to 50 seconds and are about three contractions per ten minutes</li>
<li>The uterus should always relax between contractions</li>
<li>The cervix dilates progressively from 4cm at a rate of approximately 1cm and 1.5cm hourly in primigravida and multigravida respectively</li>
<li>The descent of the presenting part can be noted by abdominal palpation or vaginal examination. Avoid unnecessary vaginal examination</li>
</ul>
<p>Remember to allay the mother's fears and reassure her throughout your examination and indeed throughout labour. A mother in labour needs to feel loved, cared for and to be treated with dignity.</p>
<p>Prevention of Infection</p>
<p>To prepare for clean delivery you should:</p>
<ul>
<li>Give an enema or suppositories</li>
<li>Avoid shaving the pubic hair, given current HIV/AIDS prevalence rates</li>
<li>Allow the mother to have bath whenever she wishes as it is soothing during labour</li>
<li>Practice aseptic techniques through labour</li>
<li>Ensure a clean environment within and around the ward</li>
</ul>
<p>After conducting a thorough examination of the mother and recording your observations in the partogram, there are a number of things you can do to make her feel comfortable during her labour i.e. allow her to change position and move around, use back massage, have a chosen companion with her during labour, allow her to take fluids as required and return the placenta to parents if so desired and directed by the culture. However do not forget to check on the foetus especially if you suspect foetal distress.</p>
<p>Write down any signs of foetal distress that you can think of.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>If you diagnose foetal distress what steps might you carry out?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-second-stage-of-labour">The Second Stage of Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-second-stage-of-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As mentioned earlier, the second stage of labour begins with full dilatation of the cervix. It is the stage of descent and expulsion of the baby. It normally lasts from one to two hours on average in primigravida, and half an hour in multipara. If this stage goes beyond two hours it is considered abnormal. The contractions become stronger, lasting 40 to 60 seconds, with a one-minute recovery interval.</p>
<p>The retracted and contracting uterus pushes the foetus down into the pelvis. During the relaxation phase the pelvic floor pushes the presenting part up again but the retracted uterus does not allow all the progress achieved to be lost. Progressively the presenting part moves down until it reaches the pelvic floor. The presence of a foreign body in the vagina makes the woman want to bear down even against her will. The voluntary muscles of her abdomen and diaphragm help the uterus in the pushing. As a contraction comes the mother should be instructed to take in a deep breath, hold it and then bear down.</p>
<p>These expulsive efforts are partially voluntary. The vagina widens to accommodate the baby. It is now a continuous cavity with the uterus. The presenting part may push out faeces from the rectum as it goes through the vagina. When the presenting part reaches the pelvic floor it starts stretching the vulva, causing much pain, especially in the primigravida. For some time it keeps popping out during a contraction and receding back during the relaxation phase. After some time it can no longer slip back. This is known as 'crowning'. The head will have passed through the bony outlet of the pelvis. The perineum becomes stretched and paper-thin and it is at this stage that an episiotomy should be performed if necessary. The next contraction normally expels the presenting part.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanism-of-the-second-stage-of-labour">Mechanism of the Second Stage of Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-the-second-stage-of-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The mechanism of labour refers to a series of movements the foetus has to make to pass through the birth canal.</p>
