<li>Typically long and thin with apparently disproportionately large head</li>
<li>With skin that is often dry and peeling and there is abundant palmar and plantar skin creases</li>
<li>Described as looking like 'worried old men' (it has been suggested that having been starved in utero, they are worried that this pattern is going to continue)</li>
<li>Active in behaviour, indicating from early age that they are hungry</li>
</ul>
<p>Despite their low weight for their gestational age, the length of this type of baby is less affected and the occipito frontal head circumference may well be within normal range for a term infant. This group demonstrates an asymmetrical growth pattern.</p>
<p>The second group of infants are apparently small for their gestational age but growth retardation is symmetrical. These are likely to be totally normal infants whose size is consistent with ethnic and genetic expectations, for example, small bodied parents may give birth to small babies.</p>
<p>A third group comprises those whose growth has been stunted in utero by the teratogenic effects of infection, drugs or alcohol abuse. In this group, all parameters such as weight, length and head circumference are compromised. This last group is likely to present a variety of</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>problems in the neonatal period, due to the effects of these drugs/substances.</p>
<p>In summary, therefore, the main causes of small for gestational age infants includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Maternal diseases such as hypertensive disorders</li>
<li>Placental transfer of inappropriate substances which have teratogenic effects such as nicotine, alcohol, cocaine or infective agents</li>
<li>Extremes of maternal age, that is those at either end of the childbearing spectrum, for example, very young or old parent</li>
<li>Socio-economic factors, including poor nutrition of the mother during pregnancy</li>
<li>Parity and number of foetuses in utero all impinge on the normal growth pattern</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-the-small-for-gestational-age-infant">Management of the Small for Gestational Age Infant<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-the-small-for-gestational-age-infant" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Neonatal management is planned to redress the balance or at best minimise the effects of intrauterine deprivation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="immediate-care">Immediate Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#immediate-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There is no significant difference in management at delivery from that described for the normal infant except perhaps that the small for gestational age infant would be more susceptible to hypothermia. The delivery room should, therefore, be kept warm and the infant dried and wrapped in a warm blanket or wrap.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nutritional-needs">Nutritional Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nutritional-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Since these babies have been starved in utero, as soon as they are born they are very hungry. They suck at the fingers and early and frequent feeding is called for. Since successful initiation and establishing of lactation operates by the law of supply and demand, mothers who are breast feeding may encounter a few problems, where the demand far exceeds the supply. Breast milk is ideal because in the first few days it contains high proteins and immunoglobins that the SGA infant needs. If for reasons beyond the mother's control, she should have to use formula milk, the child's caloric requirements should be calculated at a higher rate.</p>
<p>Lack of subcutaneous fat and liver glycogen stores compromise the nutritional reserves of the SGA infant. Feeds are, therefore, calculated at a rate of 90mls/kg/day increasing by 10 - 15ml/kg/day to a maximum of 200ml/kg/day.</p>
<p>Blood sugar should be monitored six hourly for 48 hours. Loss of weight is minimal in these infants, who will soon exhibit rapid weight gain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="temperature-control">Temperature Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#temperature-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The infant has a relatively mature temperature control mechanism but lack of subcutaneous fat makes them more susceptible to hypothermia from fluctuations of the environmental temperature.</p>
<p>A thermal neutral environment should be provided and the infant's temperature should be monitored regularly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="skin-care">Skin Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#skin-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Particular attention should be paid to those infants with dry, cracked and peeling skin.</p>
<p>Ensure the infant is kept clean and dry to prevent infection. After bathing the infant, apply Vaseline oil to the skin. Encourage the mother to massage her baby, which offers the additional benefits of enhancing the mother baby relationship.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications-associated-with-the-sga-infant">Complications Associated with the SGA Infant<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications-associated-with-the-sga-infant" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following complications are associated with the SGA infant:</p>
<ul>
<li>Asphyxia incidence is high but respiratory distress syndrome (RDS) rarely occurs</li>
<li>Hypoglaceamia may occur due to delayed or low glucose feeds</li>
<li>Hypothermia is common, especially if the environmental warmth is inadequate</li>
<li>There is a substantial mortality risk although the rate is lower than in preterm infants</li>
</ul>
<p>You have finished looking at the first group of high risk neonates.</p>
<p>You have seen that they include preterm and small for gestational age infants.</p>
<p>The table below will enable you to make a quick comparison of the characteristics associated with each high risk group.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Preterm</th><th>Small for Gestational Age</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Skin red and shiny, lanugo, plentiful</td><td>Skin dry and wrinkled</td></tr><tr><td>Skull bones soft,</td><td>Skull bones firm</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>sutures and fontanelles wide</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Plantar creases not visible</td><td>Plantar creases are well defined from toes to heel</td></tr><tr><td>Most of the reflexes e.g. swallowing, moro absent</td><td>Moro and traction reflexes present</td></tr><tr><td>Pinna of ears soft and flat</td><td>Pinna of ear has cartilaginous ridges and firm</td></tr><tr><td>Born before 37 weeks gestation</td><td>Born at term</td></tr><tr><td>Eyes always closed</td><td>Eyes wide open (worried look)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-post-term-infant">The Post Term Infant<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-post-term-infant" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You will now look at the characteristics of a post term infant.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="try-to-list-several-characteristics-of-a-post-term-infant">Try to list several characteristics of a post term infant.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#try-to-list-several-characteristics-of-a-post-term-infant" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A post term infant is a baby born after 294 days, that is, 40 weeks of gestation. Accurate dating and calculation of the gestation period is important. The main characteristic features of a post term infant include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Skin is loose, dry and disquamating (peeling off)</li>
<li>Skull bones are hard and firm</li>
<li>Small fontanelles and narrow sutures</li>
<li>Nails are overgrown hence long</li>
</ul>
<p>This infant is at risk of developing complications because:</p>
<ul>
<li>The placenta starts diminishing in its functions and hence the foetus may not get enough nutrients and oxygen. This will lead to asphyxia in utero and passing of meconium which will affect the infant's life.</li>
<li>Since the bones of the skull are firm, moulding is not effective and this may lead to a difficult delivery.</li>
</ul>
<p>In managing the infant, care should be taken to resuscitate the baby first in the same manner as with a preterm infant.</p>
<p>Due to inborn errors of metabolism the post term infant will not thrive, will be irritable, may have changes in stool and will suffer from colic or vomiting with weight loss. Any infant not doing well on the usual formula or breast milk should be evaluated for some type of inborn error of metabolism.</p>
<p>The two common causes of metabolic disorder in newborn post term infants are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hypoglycaemia and Hyperglycaemia</li>
<li>Hypocalcaemia and Hypercalcaemia</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hypoglycaemia">Hypoglycaemia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hypoglycaemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a condition of low blood glucose in the body. Criteria for hypoglycaemia are:</p>
<p>Full term infants - blood sugar &lt; 2.2mmol/l (40mg/dl)</p>
<p>Premature infants - blood sugar &lt; 1.1mmol/l (20mg/dl)</p>
<p>Symptoms of hypoglycaemia in the newborn can be divided into major and minor symptoms. Major symptoms include apnoea, convulsions and coma. Minor symptoms include jitteriness, irritability, tremors, apathy, cyanotic spells and temperature instability.</p>
<p>Many babies may remain asymptomatic. It is unusual for a newborn with hypoglycaemia to have an autonomic nervous system response with sweating, pallor and tachycardia as occurs in adults. It is, therefore, not possible to confidently diagnose neonatal hypoglycaemia clinically as the symptoms are so similar to infection.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Low blood sugar detected by a stick test (Dextrostix or BM sticks) should be checked by a laboratory blood essay for glucose.</p>
<p>There are several causes of hypoglycaemia. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Decreased substrate (a substance upon which an enzyme acts) availability, for example, in small for gestational age infants and premature infants</li>
<li>Increased glucose utilisation, for example, inborn errors of amino acid metabolism, glycogen storage disease, and galactosaemia</li>
<li>Infants of diabetic mothers and large for gestational age infants are also at risk of developing hypoglycaemia</li>
<li>Miscellaneous factors like birth asphyxia and endocrine deficiencies in growth hormone and cortisol</li>
</ul>
<p>The major aim of management is to prevent hypoglycaemia from developing. However, if it does occur, it should be detected early, before symptoms are fully apparent.</p>
<p>The following steps should be taken:</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ol>
<li>Monitor blood glucose frequently depending on previous blood glucose levels.</li>
<li>Early and frequent feeding leads to good prognosis in asymptomatic hypoglycaemia. In diabetic infants hypoglycaemia can be prevented.</li>
<li>Treatment should be initiated without any delay and plasma glucose level rechecked directly to ensure that the problem is resolved.</li>
<li>10 - 20% dextrose should be administered intravenously and carefully monitored because should it stop suddenly, because of extravasation into the tissues, there is risk of reactive hypoglycaemia.</li>
<li>If there is difficulty or delay in starting the infusion, then intramuscular glucagon can be given.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prognosis">Prognosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prognosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infants with asymptomatic hypoglycaemia are usually normal at follow up assessment. Some of the babies with major symptoms of hypoglycaemia die or have a severe neurological handicap at follow up. The problems they may have include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mental retardation</li>
<li>Convulsion</li>
<li>Spasticity and microcephaly</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hyperglycaemia">Hyperglycaemia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hyperglycaemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is usually defined as blood sugar levels greater than 9 mmol/l at which level glycosuria may occur.</p>
<p>It frequently occurs in an infant who is receiving 10% dextrose infusion or parenteral nutrition. Hyperglycaemia usually responds to decreasing the glucose concentration or the infusion rate. Hyperglycaemia must be considered to be a sign of septicaemia. In management, soluble insulin 0.1 units per kg body weight should be given intravenously and repeated as necessary to keep blood sugar below 9 mmol/l.</p>
<p>Next you will look at other deficiencies closely related to hypoglycaemia and hyperglycaemia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hypocalcaemia">Hypocalcaemia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hypocalcaemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is usually defined as a serum calcium concentration of less than 1.8 mmol/l (7.5mg/dl). The newborn rarely develops symptoms unless the plasma calcium is lower than 1.7 mmol/l.</p>
<p>The condition usually occurs within 72 hours of life in the following situations:</p>
<ul>
<li>Premature neonates</li>
<li>Associated with RDS</li>
<li>Birth asphyxia</li>
<li>Infants of diabetic mothers</li>
<li>Neonatal sepsis</li>
</ul>
<p>The reasons why it occurs are not fully understood but persistent hypocalcaemia is due to hypoparathyroidism.</p>
<p>This is a rare condition and may be inherited in either x-linked or autosomal recessive manner. Late hypocalcaemia is often referred to as neonatal tetany and occurs after the first week of life. In the past it was commonly a result of feeding the infant on unmodified cow's milk. Late hypocalcaemia, which is rare, is due to maternal hyperparathyroidism.</p>
<p>Hypocalcaemia can cause tetany, jitteriness and convulsions, as well as seizures, which may be identical to those due to hypoglycaemia or other cerebral causes. Occasionally bradycardia and apnoea may also result from hypocalcaemia.</p>
<p>The condition can be prevented by giving 10% Calcium Gluconate in intravenous infusion to ill infants, who are not taking oral feeds. Observe the infusion site carefully as extravasation may cause severe tissue injury.</p>
<p>Severe and resistant hypocalcaemia such as that which occurs in congenital parathyroidism may require Vitamin D supplementation. Some cases of hypocalcaemia will not respond to calcium gluconate infusion and require magnesium sulphate.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prognosis">Prognosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prognosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most infants recover completely with no adverse neurodevelopmental consequences. Severe dental caries may be seen in the primary dentition of some infants with tetany due to hypocalcaemia. Remember that calcium is essential to the development of bones including teeth.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hypercalcaemia">Hypercalcaemia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hypercalcaemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This may occur as a complication of calcium overdose. Usually, serum calcium is greater than 2.75mmol/l, which is often due to excessive use of calcium gluconate in intravenous fluid therapy.</p>
<p>Other causes include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Renal failure</li>
<li>Inappropriate anti-diuretic hormone secretion</li>
<li>Hyperparathyroidism</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Vitamin D intoxication</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="neonatal-jaundice">Neonatal Jaundice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neonatal-jaundice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is one of the most common problems encountered by the newborn. Elevated levels of unconjugated bilirubin lead to the most common form of neonatal jaundice. If this passes through the blood brain barrier it can cause permanent brain damage, with chronic disability.</p>
<p>Jaundice is a condition in which there is yellow discolouration of skin, mucus membranes and body fluids occurring during the first 28 days of life due to deposition of bilirubin.</p>
<p>The metabolism of bilirubin</p>
<p>RBC (Broken down in Reticulo-endothelial system)</p>
<p>IRON + BILIRUBIN (Indirect-unconjugated â€“ soluble in fat.)</p>
<p>Combines with ALBUMIN and is carried to the LIVER.</p>
<p>LIVER-Y-PROTEINS takes over from ALBUMIN.</p>
<p>GLUCURONIC ACID acts on BILIRUBIN with GLUCURONIC-TRANSFARES which acts as a CATALYST (This enzyme is not present in a premature liver)</p>
<p>DIRECT (CONJUGATED) BILIRUBIN (water soluble)</p>
<p>Passes through BILE DUCT.</p>
<p>To be Stored in Gall bladder to be used in digestion of fat.</p>
<p>Whatever is not used is excreted through faeces (STERCOBILINOGEN)</p>
<p>Due to obstructions in the liver, the bile is forced back into circulation. It may also be recirculated if there is a delay in the excretion of stercobilinogen. Bilirubin from the blood circulation is excreted through the kidneys and is called urobilinogen. Obstructive jaundice stool is pale and may occur as early as the second week of life of the infant.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="try-to-list-three-causes-of-neonatal-jaundice">Try to list three causes of neonatal jaundice.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#try-to-list-three-causes-of-neonatal-jaundice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>I hope your answer included some of the following causes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Physiological changes</li>
<li>Rhesus incompatibility</li>
<li>ABO incompatibility</li>
<li>Infections</li>
<li>Obstruction in the bile duct or liver</li>
</ul>
<p>Next you are going to look at the different types of jaundice.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physiological-jaundice">Physiological Jaundice<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physiological-jaundice" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Jaundice is found in 50% of mature babies, while 75% of pre-term babies are affected.</p>
<p>The condition is common in a newborn especially the preterm, particularly after oxytocin stimulated labour and when breast feeding is initiated (it has been shown that some women excrete a steroid, known as pregnanediol, which inhibits liver conjugation). During foetal life in utero, the Hb is high (14 - 18 mg/100mls). The excess red blood cells are no longer required by the body after birth and hence are destroyed. The Hb is reduced when the red cells are broken down. However, the liver cells are not mature enough to cope with the breakdown rate and, therefore, there is a high level of bilirubin in the blood.</p>
<p>This condition occurs between the third to sixth day of life and disappears within the seventh to tenth day of life. It may take longer in premature babies.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-06ab1dc4.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg">
Jaundice ratios in mature and pre-term babies</p>
<p>Physiological jaundice does not do any harm to the child in the majority of cases. In rare cases, where the jaundice is marked, there may be a little sluggishness or refusal to feed on the part of the infant for some time.</p>
<p>The infant should be given extra fluids with glucose and the mother should be encouraged to breast feed. If the condition persists then further investigations are recommended,</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>although it normally disappears without any treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="jaundice-due-to-rhesus-incompatibility">Jaundice Due to Rhesus Incompatibility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#jaundice-due-to-rhesus-incompatibility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is also known as haemolytic disease of the newborn. The Rhesus factor is an antigen found in the red blood cells of about 83% of the population. Approximately 17% of the population is said to be rhesus negative. Problems arise if a Rhesus negative mother and a Rhesus positive father have a child who inherits the Rhesus positive traits of the father.</p>
<p>In this case, the child will receive a chromosome with the recessive gene 'd' from the Rhesus negative mother and another with the dominant gene 'D' from the Rhesus positive father. The later chromosome can be problematic. The chromosome is foreign to the mother, and should the foetal blood escape into maternal circulation, it will stimulate the formation of antibodies by the mother.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-a276fcf5.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg">
Rhesus incompatibility</p>
<p>The first born is not usually affected unless the mother had previously undergone an abortion. During the third stage of labour, when the placenta is being delivered, the maternal and foetal blood mixes, and the mother's body is sensitised against the baby's blood. There are antibodies circulating in the mother's blood due to this sensitisation.</p>
<p>If the mother conceives again and the foetus is Rhesus positive, then the antibodies circulating in the mother's blood will cross to attack the Rhesus positive genes in the foetus. The blood cells are destroyed, causing anaemia and excess bilirubin in the blood of the baby. The baby will consequently be born with jaundice, known as Kernicterus gravis neonatorum, which is normally seen 12 - 24 hours after delivery. Sometimes the neonate may not appear jaundiced, but the amniotic fluid will be golden and the cord is yellow.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The most important gene in Rhesus incompatibility is the dominant D. Persons possessing this gene are commonly termed Rhesus positive. When it is absent from both chromosomes and its place is occupied by the recessive d, the individual is termed negative.</p>
<p>Since early detection may save the infant from developing more serious complications, it is important to understand how the condition presents.</p>
<p>The classic presentation of Kernicterus in a newborn is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Progressive development of lethargy</li>
<li>Rigidity</li>
<li>Opisthotonus (a form of spasm in which the head and the heels are bent backwards and the body bowed forward)</li>
<li>High pitched cry</li>
<li>Fever and convulsions</li>
<li>In 50% of affected infants, death follows</li>
<li>At autopsy, there is bilirubin staining and necrosis of neurons, especially in the basal ganglia, hippocampus and subthalmic nuclei</li>
</ul>
<p>For those infants who do survive, kernicterus will often present with:</p>
<ul>
<li>Cerebral palsy</li>
<li>High frequency deafness</li>
<li>Mental retardation</li>
<li>Paralysis of upward gaze (parinaud's sign) where the baby looks upward permanently</li>
</ul>
<p>Preterm infants may manifest more serious bilirubin brain damage. High frequency hearing loss is the commonest feature of the bilirubin encephalopathy syndrome and is most commonly seen in premature infants. Acidosis, asphyxia, prematurity and drugs which compete for bilirubin binding sites, predispose infants to kernicterus possibly by opening the blood brain barrier to bilirubin molecules.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>In the most severe form of jaundice, the baby is oedematous, has fluid retention in the body and may have cardiac failure. This baby is not usually saved. The term 'hydrops foetalis' is used to describe this foetus, which is usually a dead macerated infant. The placenta is usually large and oozing a lot of fluid.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The aim of management is to save the unborn baby.</p>
<p>During antenatal care, profile blood is taken for grouping and Hb and the following steps should also be taken:</p>
<ol>
<li>A mother who is Rhesus negative should be closely observed and at 26 - 28 weeks gestation blood is taken for Coomb's test, which is repeated at 34 - 36 weeks.</li>
<li>Antibody titre is estimated. If it continues to rise, then labour is induced.</li>
<li>Blood transfusion exchange can be done in utero (this is possible in more developed countries).</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="care-during-labour">Care During Labour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#care-during-labour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Labour may be induced by the 36th - 37th week. Sedatives and anaesthetic drugs should be given with restraint, since this is a high risk baby. Preparations should be made for blood exchange transfusion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="care-at-birth">Care At Birth<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#care-at-birth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The cord blood is taken immediately at birth for Haemoglobin (Hb) and Coomb's test and serum bilirubin. A bilirubin level of above 4 mg/100mls and Hb of below 14g/100 mls is an indication that a severe haemolytic process has already started. Blood exchange is urgently necessary. Normal bilirubin is 2gm/100ml.</p>
<p>The cord should be cut as soon as possible after birth to prevent further increase of blood from the placenta. The length of the cord should be at least 3 - 4 inches (7.6 - 10.2cm) to facilitate its use for blood exchange transfusion. Wrap the cord with gauze soaked in saline solution to prevent drying. Weigh the baby when it is born as this will guide you in the calculation of dosages of drugs and blood for exchange.</p>
<p>Note the following points, which will guide you on when blood transfusion exchange is needed:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Bilirubin level rising at the rate of 1mg per hour where the mother is Rhesus negative</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A drop in Hb of 1mg per hour or 13mg or below at birth</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the bilirubin level is 10mgs, the baby should be put under phototherapy and exchange transfusion should be organised</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Early feeding reduces the incidence of jaundice by preventing dehydration and the elevation of free fatty acids. In addition, feeding will overcome bowel stasis, minimise the effects of the enterohepatic bilirubin circulation and increase the excretion of bilirubin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="exchange-transfusion">Exchange Transfusion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exchange-transfusion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The purpose of a blood exchange transfusion is to remove toxic bilirubin in exchange for fresh blood. The main aims of the procedure are:</p>
<ul>
<li>To remove toxic unconjugated bilirubin which has already accumulated</li>
<li>To remove the sensitised red blood cells of the neonate to prevent further haemolysis</li>
<li>To correct anaemia</li>
<li>To remove the anti Rhesus antibodies from the neonate's circulation</li>
</ul>
<p>The exchange transfusion aims at reducing the levels of bilirubin and correcting anaemia. Please note that the donor's blood must be compatible with both infant's and mother's serum.</p>
<p>The following steps should be followed when preparing for exchange transfusion:</p>
<ol>
<li>Explain the procedure to the parents and get their signed consent.</li>
<li>Get the exchange transfusion tray.</li>
<li>Prepare the equipment required for exchange transfusion, including fresh blood.</li>
<li>Ensure there is an overhead heat source to maintain room temperature at 28 - 32 degrees Celsius.</li>
<li>Starve the baby for four hours before the procedure in order to avoid over distension of the abdomen.</li>
<li>The baby should be bandaged to an across-splint.</li>
</ol>
<p>A stethoscope for frequent checking of heart rate during the procedure is strapped to the left chest wall of the infant.</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>You should have a thermometer for temperature monitoring.</li>
</ol>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Exchange Blood Transfusion Technique
Try to describe the role of the midwife during a blood exchange transfusion.</p>
<p>Did you note the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>The midwife assists in preparing the equipment, the baby and stays with the baby throughout the procedure.</li>
<li>The doctor should pass a special polyethylene catheter through the umbilical vein into the inferior vena cava.</li>
<li>10mls of blood is withdrawn for assessment of bilirubin, glucose level and haemoglobin before transfusion. If blood glucose is low, it will not be replaced to avoid overloading the circulation.</li>
<li>The doctor then starts by removing 10 - 20mls of blood.</li>
<li>This is discarded then replaced with the same amount of compatible Rhesus negative blood through the same catheter.</li>
<li>The procedure is repeated until the calculated volume has been replaced. About 300mls is exchanged at a time and the entire procedure takes about two hours.</li>
<li>The amount of blood to be given is calculated according to body weight, that is, 170ml/kg.</li>
<li>A continuous slow infusion of calcium gluconate may be given concurrently in order to counteract any hypocalcaemia due to the citrate in the stored blood.</li>
<li>The baby's condition, that is the heart rate, respiration and umbilical vein pressure (normal 4 - 8cm H2O), is monitored every 5 - 15 minutes.</li>
<li>Temperature is also monitored in order to avoid the development of hypothermia or hyperthermia.</li>
<li>Keep a record of blood injected and withdrawn and any drug administered.</li>
<li>Report any cyanosis, pallor, tachycardia, rib recession or grunting, twitching, jittery or any abnormal movements by the baby.</li>
<li>If the baby is restless, calm them by giving a stuffed teat soaked in 5 - 10% dextrose/glucose for baby to suck. The whole procedure takes about two hours and at the end, the last syringe of blood removed is preserved for post transfusion test.</li>
</ul>
<p>Care of the Infant after Transfusion</p>
<p>The infant is placed in a warm cot or incubator and observed closely quarter to half hourly for six to eight hours. Records of respiration and heart rate should be kept. The umbilical cord dressing should be checked frequently for bleeding every half hour for the first few hours, then four hourly.</p>
<p>Blood serum bilirubin levels are estimated four hourly. Capillary blood glucose should also be estimated at four hour intervals. The infant should be put under phototherapy as soon as the procedure is over because the serum bilirubin level will rebound as antibodies in the tissues return to the circulation and haemolyse further red blood cells.</p>
<p>Remember:</p>
<p>If the baby under phototherapy is passing loose stools, the baby can be removed and treatment given outside the phototherapy room.</p>
<p>If the bilirubin levels rise again instead of falling, two to eight hours after the initial exchange, a second exchange will have to be done. Meanwhile, you should reassure the parents. The rest of the care will be as for newborn infants.</p>
<p>Remember:</p>
<p>The exchange transfusion should be done early, before the serum bilirubin levels have exceeded 16mg/100mls. Once the signs of kernicterus have set in, it is too late to expect perfect results even if the exchange is given.</p>
<p>However, you should note that there are several adverse effects of the blood exchange procedure, which include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Cardiac failure (especially when the exchange is done too fast)</li>
<li>Cardiac arrest (hypoglycaemia due to decreased blood sugar levels)</li>
<li>Air embolism</li>
<li>Perforation of the intestines</li>
<li>Hypocalcaemia due to decreased level of potassium</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 136 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-of-rhesus-isoimmunisation">Prevention of Rhesus Isoimmunisation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-of-rhesus-isoimmunisation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Mothers at risk of developing Rhesus isoimmunisation are given an injection of anti D within 72 hours of delivery, especially during the first delivery. Appropriate antenatal care is essential to detect some of these risk factors. The mother should be advised to deliver in a hospital environment.</p>
<p>If kernicterus sets in as a complication, the infant will present with the signs and symptoms that have already been listed earlier.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="abo-incompatibility">ABO Incompatibility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#abo-incompatibility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Group factor incompatibility is much more common than Rhesus incompatibility but the disease is milder. There is no sensitisation and this occurs if the baby happens to be blood type A or B and the mother is O, which has antibodies for A and B.</p>
<p>Incompatibility happens if the maternal blood enters the foetus' blood and the antibody attacks. It is dependant on the blood type, which has entered the foetal circulation. Treatment is the same as for Rhesus incompatibility.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Mother's Blood Group</th><th>Infant's Blood Group</th><th>Frequency of Incompatibility</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>O</td><td>A or B</td><td>Common</td></tr><tr><td>A</td><td>B or AB</td><td>Rare</td></tr><tr><td>B</td><td>A or AB</td><td>Rare</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-of-kernicterus-in-a-jaundiced-baby">Prevention of Kernicterus in a Jaundiced Baby<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-of-kernicterus-in-a-jaundiced-baby" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The complication of kernicterus in jaundice can be prevented by performing a blood exchange to keep the serum bilirubin level below 20mg/100ml. Phototherapy to the jaundiced infant will prevent a further rise in serum bilirubin, so that kernicterus causing levels are not reached. In some hospitals, albumin is given with exchange blood transfusion in an effort to bind bilirubin with the aim of lessening brain damage. Finally, you should avoid the use of sulphonamides and salicylates in infants.</p>
<p>You have come to the end of this sub section and will now move on to infections affecting neonates.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="neonatal-infections">Neonatal Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neonatal-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You will now focus on infections that are passed on from mother to child and those that are derived from the surrounding environment.</p>
<p>Sepsis or septicaemia is the presence of micro-organisms in the blood. Infection is an ever present problem in the newborn and sepsis has always been one of the prime causes of neonatal mortality. Neonates are susceptible to infection for many reasons, for example:</p>
<ul>
<li>Their polymorphonuclear leukocytes are less efficient in phagocytosis.</li>
<li>The infant receives passive transplacental immuno-globulins from the mother, which protect against common viral infections and some bacterial infections for the first three to six months of life. However, passive immunisation from the mother does not include 1gA, 1gE or 1gM immunoglobins. The level of 1gG is much lower in premature infants.</li>
<li>The inflammatory response is less rapid and extensive than later in life.</li>
<li>The baby is bacteriologically sterile, which results in little competition from existing bacterial flora when they are exposed to pathogens.</li>
<li>The likelihood of infection is increased in the male infant for unexplained reasons.</li>
<li>The preterm and small for gestational age infants are particularly predisposed to infection.</li>
</ul>
<p>You will now look at the various origins of infections.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="intrauterine-congenital-infections">Intrauterine (Congenital) Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intrauterine-congenital-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Transplacental infections in the first trimester include toxoplasmosis, rubella and herpes simplex type II. In the second trimester, possible infections include Treponema pallidum and in the third trimester and labour, infections will include herpes zoster, hepatitis B, B-haemolytic streptococcus, Haemophilus influenzae and pneumococcus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ascending-infections">Ascending Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ascending-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>These occur after early rupture of the membranes and represent the most common form of intrauterine infection. They are often a result of repeated vaginal examinations. Causative organisms include the most frequent pathogens like escherichia coli, Klebsiella Pseudomonas, Proteus aureas and Streptococcus feacalis.</p>
<p>All of these are bowel organisms, which may have been introduced when performing vaginal examinations. Other causative organisms include group B haemolytic Streptococcus and</p>
<p>group A Streptococcus, and occasionally Staphylococcus aureus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intrapartum-infections">Intrapartum Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intrapartum-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These organisms colonise the infant as it passes through the birth canal.</p>
<p>Prolonged rupture of membranes also predisposes the neonate to intrapartum infection. Pathogens include herpes simplex II, Neisseria gonorrhoea, hepatitis B, Chlamydia Trachomatis, Candida albicans.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nosocomial-infections">Nosocomial Infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nosocomial-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are infections acquired in the hospital. They include bacterial infections, like staphylococcus, salmonella, shigella and anaerobic bacteria as well as viral infections like rotavirus, adenovirus, echovirus, coxsackie. They may also be fungal infections, for example, candida albicans.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="try-to-list-five-indicators-you-would-consider-in-detecting-an-infection-problem-in-the-infant">Try to list five indicators you would consider in detecting an infection problem in the infant.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#try-to-list-five-indicators-you-would-consider-in-detecting-an-infection-problem-in-the-infant" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>First and foremost, you should be vigilant in your observations to help in the early diagnosis and treatment of infection. Highly susceptible infants, who should be observed closely, include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Premature neonates</li>
<li>Babies born following prolonged rupture of membranes</li>
<li>Babies born following prolonged and difficult labour</li>
<li>Babies who have undergone prolonged resuscitation</li>
<li>Babies who have undergone operations for surgical correction of congenital anomalies</li>
<li>Infants born by mothers with diabetes, toxaemia of pregnancy and chronic anaemia</li>
<li>Babies whose mothers have active PTB</li>
<li>Babies delivered in unhygienic surroundings, especially those born before arrival (BBA's)</li>
<li>Bottle fed babies</li>
</ul>
<p>The presence of one or more of the following signs is very suggestive of infection:</p>
<ul>
<li>Refusal to suck</li>
<li>Vomiting</li>
<li>Diarrhoea with or without blood or mucus in stools</li>
<li>Abdominal distension</li>
<li>Irritability</li>
<li>Weak or high pitched cry</li>
<li>Listlessness or lethargy and loss of interest in the surroundings</li>
<li>Twitching of limbs or generalised convulsions</li>
<li>Diminution or absence of reflexes especially Moro or startle reflex</li>
<li>Full fontanelles</li>
<li>Alteration in rate and rhythm of respiration, especially laboured or grunting</li>
<li>Nasal discharge</li>
<li>Small red patches on the skin</li>
<li>Ecchymoses, that is, large bleeding areas on the skin, frank bleeding from the rectum and mouth</li>
<li>Presence of rash, especially pustules</li>
<li>Jaundice</li>
<li>Cyanotic attacks</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The temperature may be raised, but a LOW temperature in the presence of an infection is not uncommon in the neonates.</p>
<p>To confirm your diagnosis the following investigations may help:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hb estimation</li>
<li>WBC count</li>
<li>Urinalysis</li>
<li>Blood culture</li>
<li>Lumbar puncture</li>
<li>Malarial parasites on blood slide</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention-of-infection">Prevention of Infection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-of-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Your role as a midwife is to be observant and create a safe environment that decreases chances of the infant acquiring infections after birth.</p>
<p>â˜ Encouraging and assisting mothers with breast feeding, hence increasing the infant's immune protection.</p>
<p>â˜ Ensuring careful and frequent hand washing by all caretakers. This simple procedure remains the single most important method of preventing the spread of infection in infants.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>â˜ Adequate spacing of cots when neonates are in the nursery with other infants.
â˜ Always using individual equipment for each infant.
â˜ Avoiding any irritation or trauma to the infant's skin and mucous membranes, as intact skin provides a barrier against infection</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Discouraging visitors who have infections or who have been exposed to communicable disease, from visiting the hospital or home.</li>
<li>Isolating infected babies when absolutely essential.</li>
<li>Observing for and appropriately treating, any infection in the mother prior to the infant's birth.</li>
<li>Giving prophylaxis treatment to an infant born of mothers with infections.</li>
<li>Effective health education to the mother on importance of infection control and prevention.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="infants-with-perinatal-hiv-aids-infection">Infants with Perinatal HIV/AIDS Infection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#infants-with-perinatal-hiv-aids-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In this time of high HIV/AIDS prevalence, it is important to cover this issue. Although HIV/AIDS will be discussed in more detail in unit six, it is important to look at it here because infants are often at a high risk of infection.</p>
<p>Although HIV is transmitted in utero, neonates rarely present with AIDS. However, there may be signs of congenital infection, for example, low birth weight, small for gestational age, anaemia, jaundice, thrombocytopenia or hepatomegaly.</p>
<p>Infants may present with the severe infantile form of HIV infection with opportunistic infections, involvement of CNS, as well as evidence of cellular and human immune deficiency within six to nine months. The clinical course may vary, but with time, there is progressive immune dysfunction accompanied by clinical deterioration.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="non-specific-signs-and-symptoms-of-the-hiv-disease">Non Specific Signs and Symptoms of the HIV Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-specific-signs-and-symptoms-of-the-hiv-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The World Health Organisation's (WHO) clinical case definition for paediatric HIV disease is classified according to major and minor criteria.</p>
<p>The major criteria include:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Weight loss or failure to thrive</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Diarrhoea for more than one month</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Fever for more than one month</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The major criteria include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Weight loss or failure to thrive</li>
<li>Diarrhoea for more than one month</li>
<li>Fever for more than one month</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>HIV infection should be suspected in a child with two major and two minor clinical features from the lists above</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-a-neonate-born-to-a-mother-with-hiv">Management of a Neonate Born to a Mother with HIV<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-a-neonate-born-to-a-mother-with-hiv" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This could include prevention of mother to child transmission (PMTCT) by the use of anti retroviral drugs (nevirapine), which are administered to the mother during labour and to the infant within 72 hours of birth.</p>
<p>However, here you are mainly going to cover nursing care, as medical treatment will be dealt with in unit five of this module.</p>
<ol>
<li>Avoid breaking/bruising the baby's skin.</li>
<li>Wipe the baby dry with particular attention to the face.</li>
<li>Feed the baby within half an hour to one hour.</li>
<li>Sleep and rest should be adequate. Ensure that medical/surgical procedures are coordinated, so that they do not interfere with the rest and sleep requirements of the infant. All procedures should be done at once to allow the infant to rest.</li>
<li>Skin integrity should be maintained. The skin should remain intact, as it is prone to fungal, viral and bacterial infection. Infectious skin conditions will require barrienursing precautions.</li>
<li>Observe the body temperature frequently as this may be elevated where there is an infected skin condition and may be an indicator to the possible development of other infections.</li>
<li>Observe both baby and environmental hygiene at all times.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nutritional-status">Nutritional Status<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nutritional-status" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The Kenya PMCT Project guidelines on feeding an HIV infected baby state that:</p>
<ol>
<li>Babies should be exclusively breast fed for the first six months of life. There is early evidence that mixed feeding will increase the risk of transmission through breast milk. This is because, with mixed feeding, the delicate gastro intestinal tract is traumatised, creating avenues</li>
</ol>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>for the entry of the virus into the breast milk.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>The infant should preferably be fed on milk only for the first six months. After this, balanced complimentary foods are added to the diet.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Respect the mother's choice of infant feeding and encourage the parents to participate in decision making. Decisions made by the parents will be better upheld than the ones imposed on them.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The infant may have problems of wasting syndrome, failure to thrive, low birth weight and/or gastroenteritis. The child should have adequate diet in order to maintain body function and growth. If feeds are tolerated, provide the appropriate diet and maintain a record of dietary intake and faecal/vomit output. Measure the infant's weight regularly.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The child's fluid and electrolyte balance should be good in order to counteract dehydration due to diarrhoea.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Safety is of paramount importance as the immune dysfunction increases the infant's susceptibility to infection. Monitor vital signs to detect early infection. Ensure basic infection control procedures are applied while nursing the infant.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maintain the body temperature because the problem of pyrexia and febrile convulsions is common. Obtain and record temperature four hourly. Use tepid sponge when necessary to reduce fever.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The neonate may experience breathing problems due to respiratory infections. Monitor respiration patterns and administer medication or oxygen when necessary. Position the infant for appropriate respiratory efforts.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Implement routine immunisation as per the Ministry of Health (MOH) guidelines.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Vitamin A supplements should also be given to the infant according to MOH guidelines. However, if the infant is on commercial infant formula, there is no need for additional supplements.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The child should be observed for up to five years. Monitor growth closely, that is, monthly in the first year, then every three months in the second year.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Continuous care is needed due to the potential for reinfection.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Health workers are encouraged to use the integrated management of childhood infection (IMCI) guidelines. These have been shown to improve diagnosis and reduce mortality from common infections in children.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="congenital-syphilis">Congenital Syphilis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#congenital-syphilis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="symptoms-of-congenital-syphilis">Symptoms of Congenital Syphilis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symptoms-of-congenital-syphilis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Another mother to child infection transmission is congenital syphilis. This is seen in neonates if maternal infection occurs after the fourth month of gestation. The causative organism is Treponema pallidum, which crosses the placenta barrier.</p>
<p>Classically, the infant at birth is found to have snuffles, skin eruptions and wide spread metaphyseal bony lesions. Interstitial keratitis is a common feature of congenital infection. Other symptoms include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Enlarged liver and spleen</li>
<li>Jaundice</li>
<li>Deformities of nails</li>
<li>Alopecia, that is, loss of hair on the head</li>
<li>Pseudoparalysis</li>
</ul>
<p>Late congenital syphilis can also involve the central nervous system, bones, teeth and skin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="management-of-congenital-syphilis">Management of Congenital Syphilis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-congenital-syphilis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Treatment of maternal syphilis in pregnancy prevents infection in the infant.</p>
<p>If the mother is VDRL positive at birth, intravenous or intramuscular antibiotic treatment is commenced immediately on diagnosis and the infant should be isolated for the first 24 hours (see syndromic management in unit six).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="hepatitis-b-positive-mother">Hepatitis B Positive Mother<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepatitis-b-positive-mother" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The Hepatitis B virus (HBV) is a common cause of hepatitis worldwide. It is transmitted by direct contact with blood or body fluids from infected persons.</p>
<p>The main sources of infection for infants are maternal blood during birth and any body secretions including breast milk. Ninety percent of the infected infants will become carriers of the virus who are at risk of developing hepatocellular carcinoma in the future.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 140 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="babies-born-before-arrival-bba">Babies Born Before Arrival (BBA)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#babies-born-before-arrival-bba" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You have covered some of the conditions, especially infections in the mother, that put the infant at high risk. There is another category of infants that are at risk. These include infants born before arrival to the health facility and abandoned babies.</p>
<p>You have probably come across infants delivered on the way to the hospital or at home, who have developed some problems. Infants in this category are predisposed to several risks.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="try-to-list-what-problems-face-infants-who-are-delivered-on-the-way-to-the-hospital-or-at-home">Try to list what problems face infants who are delivered on the way to the hospital or at home.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#try-to-list-what-problems-face-infants-who-are-delivered-on-the-way-to-the-hospital-or-at-home" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some of the problems that the infant might be prone to are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Infections like pneumonia or tetanus depending on what was used to cut the cord</li>
<li>Hypothermia if born on the way, without proper baby clothes</li>
<li>Asphyxia due to inadequate resuscitation</li>
<li>Injuries due to inexperienced hands conducting the delivery</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-babies-born-before-arrival">Management of Babies Born Before Arrival<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-babies-born-before-arrival" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>On admission, take the proper history (both obstetric and mode of delivery). Keep the infant warm. Provide nutrition for the infant. They should be started on antibiotics for both prophylaxis and for treatment. Check the cord, which may not have been handled properly by the person conducting the delivery. Measure the weight and conduct a first examination to exclude any abnormalities.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="abandoned-infants">Abandoned Infants<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#abandoned-infants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You may also come across infants who are abandoned, either in or outside the hospital. There are many reasons for individuals to abandon their newborns. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Economic pressures</li>
<li>Unwanted pregnancy</li>
<li>Babies born of incest</li>
<li>Congenital malformations on the baby</li>
<li>Sick mother, for example, psychiatric disorders</li>
<li>Orphans (especially when the mother dies during delivery)</li>
<li>Other social cultural and religious factors</li>
</ul>
<p>These infants may not be sick but they may pick up an infection. Basic/preliminary investigations like blood tests for HIV, HBV and/or a full haemogram should be carried out to rule out any infections.</p>
<p>These infants should be cared for like any normal infant, while attempts are made to trace their relatives through a social worker. Arrangements for adoption, fostering or placement in children's homes should be made as soon as possible.</p>
<p>You have come to the end of the sub section on high-risk neonates. You looked at the various categories of high risk neonates, more particularly low birth weight infants, jaundiced babies and infants affected by different infections. You also looked at the issue of infants born before arrival and those abandoned</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="neonatal-emergencies">Neonatal Emergencies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neonatal-emergencies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Neonatal emergencies are conditions or situations that require prompt and accurate action by the midwife. The speed of these actions will often help determine the outcome for the baby. It is usually a matter of life and death and midwife's knowledge, skill and correct attitude go a long way in successful management of the situation.</p>
<p>You will now look at conditions that qualify as emergency situations, for example, asphyxia neonatorum, respiratory distress syndrome (RDS) and haemorrhagic diseases of the newborn among others. For all these conditions active resuscitation is necessary.</p>
<p>This subsection starts by looking at the resuscitation procedure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="resuscitation-of-a-newborn">Resuscitation of a Newborn<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#resuscitation-of-a-newborn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Resuscitation is an emergency measure taken to sustain life or to revive when life has just ceased. The aim is to establish the heart and lung function following cardio-pulmonary arrest. There are different levels of resuscitation.</p>
