<p>First level resuscitation includes wiping of the face and body and flicking the soles of the feet to stimulate the baby.</p>
<p>Second level resuscitation involves:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clearing the airways</li>
<li>Oxygen administration</li>
<li>Mouth to mouth/bag and mask</li>
<li>Intubation, that is, intermittent positive pressure ventilation (IPPV)</li>
</ul>
<p>Cardiac massage</p>
<ul>
<li>Drug administration</li>
</ul>
<p>The principles of resuscitation of a newborn differ from the general principles of resuscitation. It is easier and better to prepare for resuscitation in the delivery room. Do remember that it is frustrating to start resuscitation and discover that the vital equipment is missing. The following are the essential requirements both in the labour ward and in the newborn unit:</p>
<ul>
<li>A source of heat, for example, a radiant warmer or resuscitaire with bed located close to oxygen and suction outlets, but located away from draughts. Air conditioning outlets should also be present.</li>
<li>At least two resuscitation trays, that is, one drug tray and one for catheters of various uses. The resuscitation and drug tray should include an airway, ambu bag, laryngoscope with blade, endo tracheal tube, suction catheters, needle, syringes, strapping tapes, gloves and cord clamps.</li>
<li>Warm dry linen, stethoscope, and a wall clock are essential for the procedure.</li>
</ul>
<p>Remember:</p>
<p>All equipment should be in the neonatal sizes.</p>
<p>As previously mentioned, a drug tray is necessary. The drug tray should contain the following items:</p>
<ul>
<li>Sodium bicarbonate 8.4% or 7.5% for correction of metabolic acidosis.</li>
<li>Fifty percent dextrose for correction of hypoglacaemia (survival of the newborn infant correlates with glycogen stores).</li>
<li>Adrenaline if the heart rate is falling after several minutes of vigorous resuscitative efforts; it may be given directly by cardiac puncture and then external cardiac massage started. This drug is also a vaso-dilator.</li>
<li>Vitamin K helps in the prevention of bleeding and it is given in cases of prolonged bleeding time in the newborn, for example, haemorrhagic disorders, intracranial injury at birth, to any baby born through difficult labour and is</li>
</ul>
<p>generally recommended for all newborn infants.</p>
<ul>
<li>Aminophylline in small doses is given to aid in respiration if distress is present (3mg kg/body weight intravenously). This drug is a vaso-dilator and also stimulates the respiratory centre.</li>
<li>Lorfan 0.25mg as an antidote to pethidine.</li>
<li>Calcium gluconate can be administered intravenously and slowly to a baby who displays tetany due to calcium deficiency. It is also a cardiac muscle stabiliser.</li>
<li>Source of oxygen to be administered after clearing the airway.</li>
<li>Clean towels/wrappers to keep infant warm during resuscitation.</li>
</ul>
<p>Principles of Resuscitation</p>
<p>You should be familiar with the ABCDO of resuscitation.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-fc868ef6.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<p>Airway should be cleared either by suction or positioning the infant.</p>
<p>Breathing, that is, establish respiration if not breathing.</p>
<p>Circulation should be noted through pulse, colour of mucus membrane and heart beat. Start cardiac massage if there is no heart beat or pulse.</p>
<p>Drugs (see the section on drugs used).</p>
<p>Observations to make a diagnosis.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Keep the infant comfortable and avoid exposure. At least two skilled persons are required to carry out the resuscitation.</p>
<ol>
<li>In a normal case of vertex presentation, a well accepted practice is to clear the mouth and pharynx with a catheter. Immediately after delivery, the infant should be placed under a radiant warmer or wrapped in warm blankets.</li>
<li>The next step will depend on the condition of the neonate. All infants should be dried well to prevent hypothermia.</li>
<li>Clear the airway by removing the remaining upper airway secretions.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong>
Use short sharp sucks to prevent inhalation of mucus during the first gasp to allow the infant to obtain air. Mechanical suction should be gently done.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Administer oxygen by using a baby funnel or mask (not closely applied to the face) at two litres per minute. A stream of oxygen directed on the baby's face will stimulate respiration and provide high oxygen for the baby's first breaths. This should be applied for a short period.</li>
<li>Bagging with oxygen, if properly done, may be the only required form of resuscitation. The infant should lie flat on their back with shoulders slightly elevated and neck slightly extended. Insert the airway above the tongue and place mask around the mouth and nose, making certain that the mask fits tightly and that the neck remains extended during bagging. Rate of bagging should be 40 - 60 per minute. The operator or his assistant should listen to the lungs for heart and breath sounds.</li>
<li>In a severely depressed infant, proceed immediately with endo-tracheal intubation and/or umbilical vessel catheterisation.</li>
<li>External cardiac massage should be commenced if the heartbeat has not returned after three or four insufflations. This should be performed by an assistant leaving the primary operator free to manage ventilation.</li>
</ol>
<p>The index and middle fingers are placed on the mid thorax at the level of the sternum and the area is pressed downward and released. Total downward displacement of the chest wall should not exceed one inch. This is because excessive vigour may cause ulceration of the liver with severe blood loss.
9. Alternate the two manoeuvres. The heart should be compressed two or three times (approximately 120 times per minute) after which a breath of oxygen is given. The two procedures must not be performed together (simultaneously).</p>
<p>The 'do nots' of neonatal resuscitation:</p>
<ol>
<li>Hold the baby upside down</li>
<li>Perform routine aspiration of the airway</li>
<li>Perform gastric suction</li>
<li>Use sodium bicarbonate routinely</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Asphyxia Neonatorum</strong></p>
<p>Asphyxia is a very common word. But what does it really mean?</p>
<p><strong>Try to write down your definition of Asphyxia.</strong></p>
<p>There are various definitions that have been suggested. As you read them, see which one is closest to the one you wrote down.</p>
<p>Some of the definitions of asphyxia are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Failure to establish respiration at birth</li>
<li>Depressed APGAR scores of less than 7 at 5 minutes</li>
<li>A direct result of respiratory dysfunction, whether alteration from utero-placental function or from alterations in the neonate's ability to breathe independently</li>
</ul>
<p>Foetal asphyxia can occur any time during gestation but is more common during labour and delivery.</p>
<p>Depression of the central nervous system of the foetus is the most common basic cause for failure to breathe at birth. However there are also maternal and foetal factors.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Try to think of at least three maternal factors, and three foetal factors, that would put an infant at risk of asphyxia.</p>
<p>Maternal factors include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Diabetes mellitus</li>
<li>Isoimmunization</li>
<li>Infection</li>
<li>Third trimester bleeding</li>
<li>Pregnancy induced hypertension or chronic hypertension</li>
<li>Drug abuse</li>
<li>Abruptio placenta</li>
<li>Placenta previa</li>
<li>Oligohydramnios</li>
<li>Polyhydramnios</li>
<li>Preterm labour</li>
<li>Prolonged second stage of labour</li>
</ul>
<p>Foetal factors include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Multiple gestation</li>
<li>Congenital anomalies</li>
<li>Intra uterine growth retardation</li>
<li>Cord prolapse/cord round the neck</li>
<li>Prematurity or post-maturity</li>
<li>Malpresentation</li>
<li>Prolonged rupture of membranes</li>
<li>Operative delivery</li>
<li>Foetal distress</li>
<li>Meconium stained amniotic fluid</li>
</ul>
<p>Remember:
The Apgar score gives some indication of the severity of asphyxia but resuscitation should never be delayed. Decision to initiate resuscitation should be based on the evaluation of the infant's respiration.</p>
<p>The signs of asphyxia will follow what is elicited on the Apgar scoring.</p>
<p>Management of Asphyxia</p>
<p>Immediate care is based on the ABC principles of resuscitation (airway, breathing, circulation) to initiate neonatal physiological adjustment. After active resuscitation continue with the following care:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Keep the baby warm, noting colour, muscle tone and respiratory attempts.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Auscultate the apex beat in order to determine whether the infant is improving or deteriorating.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Handle the baby as little as possible but observe closely, every two to four hours for vital signs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Observe for signs of irritability, twitching, convulsions. If there are any, report without delay.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Oxygen should be administered if cyanosis is present.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The airway should be kept clear of mucus by frequent suction if there are any secretions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Withhold oral feeds until six to eight hours after resuscitation for baby to recover.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Give antibiotics for prophylaxis.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advise the mother to attend the clinic for regular check up of the infant, because severe asphyxia may result in permanent damage to brain cells and cause mental retardation.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Prevention of Prenatal Asphyxia</p>
<p>Think about what you could do to prevent prenatal asphyxia
â˜ Recognise high risk pregnancies early and take the appropriate action
â˜ Accurately assess gestation when in labour in order to take the relevant precautions
â˜ Assess pulmonary maturity before inducing labour if mother is to be induced
â˜ Intrapartum foetal heart rate monitoring
â˜ Active treatment of foetal distress in utero
â˜ Ensure that a paediatrician, anaesthetist or other trained person is available to resuscitate the infant if necessary
â˜ Assess foetal placental function, for example, ultrasound, foetal movement before delivery
â˜ Discourage maternal use of alcohol and drug abuse
â˜ Avoid use of respiratory depressive drugs during late first stage of labour e.g. pethidine</p>
<p>Respiratory Distress Syndrome (RDS)</p>
<p>Respiratory Distress Syndrome (RDS) or Hyaline Membrane Disease</p>
<p>This is a disease of prematurity caused by lack of pulmonary surfactant. The production of surfactant increases with gestational age. Lack of surfactant causes collapse of alveoli with each expiration. Thus, unlike in the normal lung, no residual capacity is established. For an infant with RDS, each breath is like the first, requiring high pressures to reopen collapsed alveoli.</p>
<p>Damage to the alveoli and pulmonary capillary epithelium, secondary to surfactant deficiency, causes the formation of a hyaline membrane consisting of fibrin and sloughed cells.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>This further compromises gas exchange. Improvement will be evident in three to five days. Morbidity and mortality vary depending on gestational age.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="predisposing-factors">Predisposing Factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#predisposing-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="try-to-list-four-disposing-factors-to-respiratory-distress-syndrome-rds">Try to list four disposing factors to Respiratory Distress Syndrome (RDS).<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#try-to-list-four-disposing-factors-to-respiratory-distress-syndrome-rds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Did you include some of the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Prematurity</li>
<li>Infant of a diabetic mother. The neonate could be big, but preterm</li>
<li>Antepartum haemorrhage</li>
<li>Second twin, which, if delayed in delivery, may breathe while in utero</li>
<li>Hypoxia, acidosis, shock</li>
<li>Male infants are more prone to RDS than female infants for reasons not known</li>
<li>Possibly Caesarean section neonates (many factors involved)</li>
</ul>
<p>Clinical signs may occur immediately after birth or after several hours. The infant will present with:</p>
<ul>
<li>Grunting first, then cyanosis in room air, tachypnea, pallor, chest retractions and nasal flaring</li>
<li>Decreased breath sounds and scattered rales</li>
<li>Vital signs may be altered</li>
<li>Tachycardia or bradycardia may be observed</li>
<li>Hypertension and hypothermia</li>
<li>Infant may become progressively hypotonic and oedema and oliguria also commonly develop within the first 48 hours</li>
</ul>
<p>An x-ray usually shows the fine reticular pattern often referred to as 'ground glass'. Bronchograms are seen in the lung fields. The severity of the chest x-ray may not correlate well with the clinical severity of the RDS.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If a premature birth is anticipated, administration of steroids (dexamethasone) to the mother 24 hours before delivery may be useful.</p>
<p>Full treatment will include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Immediate resuscitation.</li>
<li>Oxygen therapy, which aims at maintaining the PO2 within the normal range. This is usually done in mild cases by increasing the inspired oxygen concentration.</li>
<li>Chest physiotherapy after the second day of life in moderate and severe cases.</li>
<li>Antibiotics if infection is a possibility.</li>
<li>Appropriate fluid balance. Intravenous fluids should be given initially.</li>
<li>Blood pressure monitoring.</li>
<li>Minimal handling and nursing in the neutral environmental temperature. Infection prevention principles should be practised.</li>
<li>Surfactant replacement therapy. The two commercially prepared surfactant products that have been approved for use by the Food and Drug Administration (FDA) are Survanta, a modified natural product, and Exosurf, a synthetic product.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Dosages and methods of administration vary for the different products but both can be administered through endo-tracheal tube as per manufacturer's literature. Surfactant replacement reduces mortality by 5 - 12% in treated infants.</p>
<p>Improvement often occurs dramatically in infants with better prognoses. Other infants, usually those of smaller birth weight and lesser gestational age, require longer periods of respiratory assistance through intubation and may fail to improve as expected.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="apnoea">Apnoea<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#apnoea" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Apnoea is the cessation of respiration for 15 - 20 seconds when cyanosis, bradycardia, hypotonia or metabolic acidosis occur. It differs from periodic breathing, during which respiration ceases for 5 - 10 seconds without pathophysiological changes.</p>
<p>Periodic breathing is a normal respiration pattern in the preterm infant but should have resolved as full term age is achieved.</p>
<p>Apnoea may occur because of immaturity of the respiratory and neural systems. Any infant who becomes apnoeic should be examined for an underlying cause. It is more likely to develop in premature infants and leads to the 'sudden infant death syndrome' (SIDS).</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 145 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infants weighing less than 1750gm or at 34 weeks
of gestation are at high risk for apnoeic episodes.
They should have their cardiac and respiratory rates,
and oxygen saturations monitored continuously for
their first two weeks of life or until five to ten days
have passed without any apnoea. Apnoeic infants
should also be quickly evaluated for bradycardia and cyanosis.
Immediate gentle tactile stimulation such as rubbing the back or flicking the soles of the feet stimulates respiration in most infants.
A bag and a mask connected to an oxygen source and equipment for suctioning and intubation should be at the bedside and ready for use in mechanical ventilation.
A respiratory centre stimulant, for example, theophylline may be used with a loading dose of 5mg/kg/day in divided dose. Caffeine citrate may be used in place of theophylline to treat apnoea in premature neonates.
Anaemia, which is a common cause of apnoea in preterm infants, should be corrected.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="haemorrhagic-disease-of-the-newborn-hdn">Haemorrhagic Disease of the Newborn (HDN)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#haemorrhagic-disease-of-the-newborn-hdn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a bleeding disorder commonly due to a temporary deficiency of the specific clotting factors, factor II (prothrombin), factor VII (proconvertin), factor XI (plasma thromboplastin component) and factor X (thrombokinase).
These factors are proteins, which need vitamin K to convert them into active clotting factors. Vitamin K is poorly transferred across the placenta, which means the foetus has low stores. Any stores are quickly depleted after birth. To enable normal clotting to occur, the baby should receive dietary vitamin K1, absorption of which will require fat and bile salts.
Vitamin K2 is synthesised by the bowel's normal flora and may also assist in the conversion of the proteins to active clotting factors. Since the neonate's bowel is sterile, Vitamin K2 production is restricted until colonisation has occurred. Because of deficiency of these active clotting factors babies are susceptible to haemorrhage. The condition may manifest in the following ways:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Haematemesis</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Melaena stool</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Haematuria</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Umbilical cord bleeding</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Bleeding usually occurs within the first four days of life and may be mistaken for the 'swallowed blood syndrome'. Bleeding occurs often in the mucous membranes of the intestinal tract and in the skin, where it is evident as bruises and on puncture sites. With severe bleeding, death can occur if appropriate measures are not undertaken.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Babies who have HDN require careful investigation and monitoring to assess their need for treatment. The diagnosis is confirmed by a prolonged prothrombin time.</p>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong>
Presence of streaks of blood in the vomitus or stool of a neonate should put the midwife on the alert.</p>
<p>The following measures should be taken:</p>
<ul>
<li>Seek medical care immediately.</li>
<li>With massive bleeding, act sensibly and quickly. Take blood for investigations as well as grouping and cross matching as you wait for specialised care.</li>
<li>Disturb the infant as little as possible and keep them warm.</li>
<li>Treat for shock.</li>
<li>If there is no doctor immediately available, administer vitamin K 1mg intramuscularly or intravenously to the baby after obtaining blood for investigations.</li>
<li>Keep all soiled articles for assessment.</li>
<li>Take blood for grouping and cross matching and prepare for transfusion (whole blood) if in hypovolaemic shock.</li>
<li>In absence of haematemesis, feed the baby but if haematemesis is present, it is advisable to withhold the feed and give intravenous fluids.</li>
<li>Maintain close observation for colour, pulse rate and bleeding sites. Note abnormal constituents of stools and vomitus.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong>
Prophylactically, Vitamin K 0.5 - 1mg by injection is recommended for all newborn infants.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 146 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="birth-trauma-and-congenital-abnormalities">Birth Trauma and Congenital Abnormalities<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#birth-trauma-and-congenital-abnormalities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In this sub section you are going to look at some of the injuries sustained by the infant during labour or at delivery.</p>
<p>Some of these injuries may occur despite skilled obstetric care. However, in recent years serious birth injuries have become relatively uncommon, a reflection of improvements in perinatal care.</p>
<p>You will need to revise the diagrams on foetal skull as well as the landmarks. You are also going to look at a few of the abnormalities covered in paediatric care.</p>
<p>Birth injuries can be split into the following groups:</p>
<ul>
<li>Injuries to the head</li>
<li>Intracranial injuries</li>
<li>Peripheral nerve injuries</li>
<li>Fractures of the long bones</li>
<li>Organ injuries</li>
</ul>
<p>The abnormalities include Down's syndrome and abnormalities due to substance abuse by the mother during pregnancy.</p>
<p>In this sub section you are going to look at some of the injuries sustained by the infant during labour or at delivery.</p>
<p>Some of these injuries may occur despite skilled obstetric care. However, in recent years serious birth injuries have become relatively uncommon, a reflection of improvements in perinatal care.</p>
<p>You will need to revise the diagrams on foetal skull as well as the landmarks. You are also going to look at a few of the abnormalities covered in paediatric care.</p>
<p>Birth injuries can be split into the following groups:</p>
<ul>
<li>Injuries to the head</li>
<li>Intracranial injuries</li>
<li>Peripheral nerve injuries</li>
<li>Fractures of the long bones</li>
<li>Organ injuries</li>
</ul>
<p>The abnormalities include Down's syndrome and abnormalities due to substance abuse by the mother during pregnancy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="injuries-to-the-head">Injuries to the Head<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#injuries-to-the-head" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Injuries to the head may vary from minor skin abrasions to severe internal haemorrhage.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-fa3c4c87.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="caput-succedaneum">Caput Succedaneum<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#caput-succedaneum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is characterised by oedema of the subcutaneous layers of the scalp. The oedema lies</p>
<p>over the presenting part of the head as it passes through the birth canal. The swelling is maximal at birth and resolves within a few days. A localised caput or chignon may be produced if deliverywas aidedbyvacuum</p>
<p>extraction.</p>
<p>to</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The swelling is formed by oedema of the structureyingsuperficial</p>
<p>the pericranium. It is not limited to one bone.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="cephalhematoma">Cephalhematoma<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cephalhematoma" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is sub periosteal haematoma, which most commonly lies over one of the parietal bones. Spontaneous absorption occurs, but this may take</p>
<p>several weeks. No treatment is necessary.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-30b525cf.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="skull-fracture">Skull Fracture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#skull-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is rarely seen nowadays. Skull fractures used to occur in a parietal or frontal bone as a result of difficult forceps delivery. However, forceps delivery is now very rarely used. If it does happen, it is best to seek neuro surgical advice about the management.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="intracranial-injuries">Intracranial Injuries<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intracranial-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Although traumatic intracranial injuries have become rare, they may occur following difficult instrumental deliveries of infants with malposition or breach deliveries. Preterm infants are particularly vulnerable, due to fragile bones.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="try-to-think-of-two-causes-for-intracranial-injuries">Try to think of two causes for intracranial injuries.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#try-to-think-of-two-causes-for-intracranial-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Direct causes include the tearing of the intracranial membranes containing blood vessels. The membranes are the falx cerebri, that is, a fold of duramater which dips down between the cerebrum and cerebellum.</p>
<p>Indirect causes include anoxia leading to venous engorgement on the skull and rapid or an abnormal type of moulding caused by prolonged or obstructed labour or by instrumental delivery.</p>
<p>Infants associated with the following conditions are also prone to intracranial injuries:</p>
<ul>
<li>Premature labour</li>
<li>Face to pubes presentation</li>
<li>Breech delivery</li>
<li>Asphyxia neonatorum</li>
</ul>
<p>Signs of intracranial injury that will be manifested depend on the severity of the condition. On the other hand, the infant may not show all the signs.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-0f4edc1f.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg"></p>
<p>At birth some babies may cry and seem normal, then later show signs of cerebral haemorrhage. A few infants are born in a state of white asphyxia. They are shocked and lie limply in the cot after responding to resuscitation. The signs include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Colour is pale</li>
<li>Respirations show abnormal variations and the condition of the infant deteriorates with increasing depression of respiratory and other vital functions due to increasing cerebral haemorrhage</li>
<li>The infant has a high-pitched cry</li>
<li>In many instances, the condition gradually improves</li>
</ul>
<p>Later signs of cerebral irritation may appear twelve hours after birth and are rarely seen later than the second or third day after delivery. They include:</p>
<ul>
<li>An infant who was limp and quiet after birth becomes restless. The child may be heard to utter a high-pitched, shrill or even piercing cry.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 147 of 317
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>The facial expression is that of an unnaturally wide awake baby.</li>
<li>The brow is wrinkled, showing a deep frown, which increases with disturbance or movement.</li>
<li>The infant is irritable and cannot tolerate handling, bright lights and sudden noise.</li>
<li>The degree of muscular twitching or rigidity of the limbs varies. Stiffness of the neck or actual head retraction may be present.</li>
<li>Sucking and swallowing reflexes are poor, with refusal to feed, or regurgitation during or after feeds.</li>
<li>Temperature may be subnormal or high.</li>
<li>Anterior fontanel may be bulging or feel tense on palpation.</li>
<li>Vomiting, convulsions and sudden phases of intense cyanosis may occur in serious cases.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="periventricular-haemorrhage">Periventricular Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#periventricular-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is predominantly a problem of preterm infants. The bleeding is around the ventricle. It is rarely seen in term infants but may accompany trauma and pre-natal hypoxia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="subdural-haemorrhage">Subdural Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#subdural-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This condition may occur following trauma during the delivery of the preterm infant when there is increased compliance of the skull and a prolonged or precipitous delivery, especially with abnormal presentations. It results from damage to the superficial veins where the great vein of Galen and the inferior sagittal sinus combine to form the straight sinus. It may also result from laceration of the tentorium and falx, particularly at their junction. It is rarely seen in term infants.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="subarachnoid-haemorrhage">Subarachnoid Haemorrhage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#subarachnoid-haemorrhage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This haemorrhage is of venous origin. There is bleeding from the veins within the subarachnoid space, which may follow mild trauma or asphyxia at birth. The condition may be asymptomatic but can present with irritability or seizures during the first few days. Later hydrocephalus may develop.</p>
<p>To diagnose, CSF is uniformly blood stained. A CT scan is also required for diagnosis. Most infants require no treatment and their long term prognosis is good. It is only in rare instances of massive haemorrhage that the prognosis is poor.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infants born in a state of white asphyxia should be resuscitated and wrapped in warm clothing for transfer to hospital if in a health centre. In long term care, the baby is placed in a room selected for its quietness, warmth and protection from direct light and draughts. Clear the airway of excess mucus and keep the head slightly raised to avoid obstruction of the airway. The baby should be handled gently and quietness maintained where possible.</p>
<p>The type and method of feeding should be made according to the condition and ability of the individual baby. The infant's temperature should be taken and recorded four hourly and tepid sponging should be done if the baby has pyrexia. The infant's linen should be changed, and toilet needs met in the cot every three to four hours. Oxygen therapy, may be necessary so keep oxygen at hand.</p>
<p>Many of these infants make good progress in the neonatal period, but not until much later is it possible to assess the degree of mental damage which has resulted from the trauma at birth. Therefore, they should be referred to the paediatric clinic for follow up.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-of-the-long-bones">Fractures of the Long Bones<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-the-long-bones" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are uncommon nowadays. The clavicle is the bone most frequently broken. Injuries are usually noticed when the infant fails to move one arm as freely as the other because of the pain. Sometimes a lump can be felt over the bone from callus formation.</p>
<p>Other fractures, which can happen although uncommon, are fractures of the femur, usually humorous, during intrauterine manipulations.</p>
<p>Orthopaedic advice should be obtained for their management. Healing is usually rapid and complete.</p>

Bishop's Computers</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="peripheral-nerve-injuries">Peripheral Nerve Injuries<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#peripheral-nerve-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nerve injuries in the newborn infant may be due to stretching, compression, twisting, hyperextension or separation of the nervous tissue. All these are as a result of complicated labour and delivery.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="facial-nerve">Facial Nerve<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#facial-nerve" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is damage to the facial nerve by pressure from the tip of the forceps. Palsy can also follow an apparent normal delivery. You will find that, on the affected side, the infant is unable to shut the eye completely. The corner of the mouth drops and the nasal-labia fold is less marked than on the unaffected side. When the infant cries, the mouth is drawn to the normal side. In most cases recovery occurs spontaneously within a few days.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="brachial-nerve-palsy">Brachial Nerve Palsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#brachial-nerve-palsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Injury to the brachial plexus may be due to excessive lateral flexion, rotation or traction upon the neck. This usually occurs during a difficult delivery, either from shoulder dystocia or</p>
<p>a breech presentation. The clinical picture depends on the nerve roots involved.</p>
<p>If the roots of C5 and C6 are damaged, then Erb's palsy results (upper brachial plexus injury). The arm lies on the side of the trunk with internal rotation at the shoulder. The elbow is extended, there is pronation of the forearm and flexion at the wrists. Klumpke's palsy (lower brachial plexus palsy) occurs when the wrist and the finger flexors are affected resulting in a claw hand deformity with wrist drop and limb fingers the upper arm is unaffected. The spinal root C8 and T1 are affected.</p>
<p>However, these injuries are increasingly uncommon, given improvements in reproductive health care. There is no treatment for brachial plexus injuries. However, passive movements of the joints will prevent contractures. Mild injuries recover within a few days, while more severe lesions can be expected to recover spontaneously in two to four months. Occasionally, there is incomplete recovery.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="radial-nerve-injury">Radial Nerve Injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#radial-nerve-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This type of injury is rare but may result from fracture of the humerus, especially with difficult delivery of the arm during breech extraction. The use of the 'deltoid region' as a site for intramuscular injection (now usually avoided) results in a radial nerve injury, because the deltoid muscle of the newborn infant is so small that correct localisation of the needlepoint is difficult.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="organ-injuries">Organ Injuries<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#organ-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following injuries to the internal organs may occur, especially with difficult deliveries like breech extraction:</p>
<ul>
<li>Liver and spleen may rupture, especially when there is hepatosplenomegaly (rhesus haemolytic disease, diabetic infants)</li>
<li>Adrenal haemorrhages may occur, but are usually a post mortem finding, being unsuspected in live infants</li>
<li>Kidneys may rupture during delivery in breech preterm infants</li>
<li>Testicles may be bruised and haemorrhages commonly seen in breech presentation</li>
</ul>
<p>You might have realised by now that difficult deliveries will lead to complications. That is why</p>
<p>there is a need for diligent observation and for early intervention in order to avoid or prevent injury to the newborn infant. You may have realised that there is very little in the form of treatment. Thus, prevention is better than cure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="congenital-abnormalities">Congenital Abnormalities<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#congenital-abnormalities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Congenital abnormalities are structural deformations present at birth.</p>
<p>Approximately 1.5% of babies are born with congenital abnormalities, especially congenital heart disease, which becomes evident when the baby is older.</p>
<p>A number of malformations are now recognised prenatally from routine ultrasound scanning or prenatal testing of the foetus.</p>
<p>When a baby is born with a defect, the parents are shocked and upset and ask for reasons. Thus, it is important to have an overview of the events that can change the normal pattern of foetal growth and development so that you can counsel the parents.</p>
<p>Most of these defects have unknown causes but are believed to be due to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Genetics</li>
<li>Adverse effects of drugs on the foetus</li>
<li>Radiation, metabolic disturbances in the mother</li>
<li>Lack of space for the foetus to grow, for example, oligohydramnios</li>
<li>Infections affecting the mother</li>
</ul>
<p>In this section you will only cover chromosome disorders. The other disorders, such as gastrointestinal tract and central nervous system malformation, have been covered in module one. You will also look at the effects of substance abuse in the development of the foetus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chromosome-disorders">Chromosome Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chromosome-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Every cell in the body contains 46 chromosomes (diploid number) in its nucleus. Forty-four are autosomes (xx or xy). Half (the haploid number) are derived from each parent at the time of fertilisation. During meiosis, the chromosomes separate and align themselves around the centre of the cell and half migrate into each daughter cell. Occasionally, one of the chromosomes does not separate in time (non-disjunction) and stays with its partner in one daughter cell, leaving the other daughter cell with no chromosomes of this type. Sometimes a process known as translocation occurs where part of one chromosome is added to another during the crossover process of meiosis.</p>
<p>Specific chromosomal abnormalities, which have been identified, include: For the purposes of your study, you will only cover the first one.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Trisomy 21</th><th>Downâ€™s syndrome</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Trisomy 14</td><td>Patau syndrome</td></tr><tr><td>Trisomy 18</td><td>Edwardâ€™s syndrome</td></tr><tr><td>xo syndrome</td><td>Turner syndrome, ovarian dysgenesis</td></tr><tr><td>xxy syndrome</td><td>Klinefelter syndrome</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b1f946d1">Downâ€™s Syndrome (Trisomy 21)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b1f946d1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downâ€™s syndrome is the commonest chromosomal disorder occurring in 1.4 per 1000 live births. It results from the presence of 47 chromosomes since the infants have three number 21 chromosomes. The majority (95%) are the result of non dysfunction during mitosis. The risk increases with maternal age from 1:350 at 35 years to 1:100 at 40 years and 1:30 at 45 years. Another 3 - 4 percent are due to translocation and are not related to maternal age.</p>
<p>If translocation affects the mother, there is a 20% risk of an affected child. If it affects the father the risk is about 6%. One or two percent are mosaic, that is, with a normal cell line as well as a cell line with an extra 21 chromosome. Chromosome analysis can be done on samples obtained by chorionic villus sampling, or amniocentesis in women at increased risk because of maternal age, or where either parent has a chromosomal translocation.</p>
<p>The characteristics of Downâ€™s syndrome are numerous and include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Eyes which slant upwards and outwards with epicanthic folds slit eyes</li>
<li>A round face, small mouth with protruding tongue and a shorter upper lip</li>
<li>Low set ears, flat occiput</li>
<li>Palpable third fontanelle</li>
<li>Hands which are short with incurring of the little finger and single transverse palmar crease (simian crease)</li>
<li>The cleft between the great toe and the others is great.</li>
<li>Profound hypotonic</li>
<li>Increased incidence of duodenal atresia and congenital heart disease</li>
<li>Many affected individuals tend to have a low intelligence quotient (IQ)</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>The survival of a baby with this disorder depends on associated malformation but survival rates are usually good. In management, a multi professional approach will be needed because these children have many needs to be addressed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drug-or-substance-addicted-infants">Drug or Substance-Addicted Infants<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drug-or-substance-addicted-infants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If a mother abuses drugs during her pregnancy, the infant may be born with withdrawal symptoms, known as Neonatal Abstinence Syndrome (NAS). These drugs include narcotics, alcohol, barbiturates, codeine, benzodiazepines, cocaine and tricyclics antidepressant. The abuse of such drugs is associated with foetal and neonatal deaths, prematurity and intrauterine growth retardation. There is also an increased risk of sudden infant death syndrome (SIDS).</p>
<p>According to Ellwood and others (1987), the risk of SIDS is increased five fold by maternal narcotic addiction. Pregnant drug abusers often have chaotic lifestyles with accompanying social and economic problems. Hence they are at more risk of STIs/HIV and Hepatitis B virus. Therefore, this puts the mother and baby in the risk group.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="neonatal-abstinence-syndrome-nas">Neonatal Abstinence Syndrome (NAS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neonatal-abstinence-syndrome-nas" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As mentioned earlier, infants born to drug abusers are born with withdrawal symptoms. You should, therefore, look out for the following signs of withdrawal:</p>
<ul>
<li>Tremor, high pitched cry, irritability</li>
<li>Hyperactivity, hypertonicity</li>
<li>Sweating, pyrexia, convulsions</li>
<li>Sneezing, vomiting, disorganised sucking</li>
<li>Diarrhoea</li>
<li>Respiratory distress</li>
</ul>
<p>In opiate misuse, the symptoms of NAS will generally appear within the first 24 â€“ 48 hours of life, but they have been reported to take up to six days to appear. Methadone and barbiturates abstinence signs may not appear for two weeks and may go up to a period of several weeks or months.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nursing-care">Nursing Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infants should be nursed in a quiet environment with reduced light and noise stimulus. Cuddle the baby so that it feels secure and give small frequent feeds for comfort and adequate nutrition. Control convulsions by giving phenobarbitone syrup. Give intravenous fluids if feeds are not tolerated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medical-treatment">Medical Treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medical-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This varies in different countries depending on the drug misused and severity of presenting signs. However, the following may be used:</p>
<ul>
<li>Narcotics, giving 0.05mg/kg of morphine every 3 - 4 hours, increasing dosage if necessary until the observable signs are controlled (Roberton, 1993).</li>
<li>In the UK the sedative chlorpromazine is also used starting at 3mg/kg/24 hours in divided doses increasing dose until presenting signs are controlled.</li>
</ul>
<p>The midwife's role includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Build up a good working relationship with the mother</li>
<li>Communication should be clear and non-judgmental</li>
<li>Explain the baby's presenting signs and emphasise that the behaviour is not a rejection of their parents</li>
<li>Encourage parents to take active part in the care of their baby</li>
<li>Emphasise the possible effects of breast feeding, which would be harmful especially with cocaine and heroine. Parents should seek advice from pharmacist or paediatrician</li>
<li>On discharge, you should coordinate the follow up and refer to appropriate professionals for rehabilitation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="foetal-alcohol-syndrome-fas">Foetal Alcohol Syndrome (FAS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foetal-alcohol-syndrome-fas" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is clear that heavy alcohol use results in the foetal alcohol syndrome. Alcohol also affects brain cells size, resulting into a smaller mass of tissue. FAS is characterised by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Intra uterine growth retardation</li>
<li>Failure to thrive</li>
<li>Developmental delay and dysmorphic facial features</li>
<li>It is thought that ethanol (a component of alcohol) disrupts cell differentiation and growth in the foetus and also impairs normal placental function</li>
</ul>
<p>The infant, therefore, will present with the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Small eyes with exaggerated epicanthic folds</li>
<li>Shallow or absent philtrum with a poorly formed nasal bridge</li>
<li>Ears that appear large</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>The infant may be fretful and difficult to feed</li>
<li>Other associated abnormalities of the heart and musculoskeletal system, gut atresia, skin lesions and cleft palate</li>
<li>Delay in mental and motor development and hence learning and behavioural problems</li>
</ul>
<p>The midwife's role is to identify women at risk and give effective antenatal education on effects of structural defects that occur during period of organogenesis. It is important for the pregnant woman to know that these defects are irreversible. You should also monitor signs of alcohol withdrawal and prepare for follow up in the community after discharge.</p>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong>
Improved nutrition, decreasing cigarette use and abstinence from alcohol greatly improve the foetal brain's chances of growing.</p>
<p>Remember that other drugs/substances such as tobacco, caffeine and cortisones that are abused during pregnancy can also lead to a foetal problem. You should now be able to identify a newborn with Down's syndrome and problems that result from substance abuse and take the appropriate action.</p>
